(2006)\n* ''[[The Churchill Factor]]'' (2014)}}\n}}\n| deputy5             = {{plainlist|\n* [[Richard Barnes (British politician)|Richard Barnes]]\n* [[Victoria Borwick]]\n* [[Roger Evans (London politician)|Roger Evans]]\n}}\n| occupation          = {{hlist|Politician|author|journalist}}\n}}\n{{Stack end}}\n{{Boris Johnson sidebar}}\n'''Alexander Boris de Pfeffel Johnson'''  ({{IPAc-en|\u02c8|f|\u025b|f|\u0259l}};<ref name=\"Who Do You Think You Are?\">{{cite episode |title=Boris Johnson |series=Who Do You Think You Are? |network=[[BBC]] |date=20 August 2008}}</ref> born 19 June 1964) is a British politician, writer and journalist who served as [[Prime Minister of the United Kingdom]] and [[Leader of the Conservative Party (UK)|Leader of the Conservative Party]] from 2019 to 2022. He previously served as [[Foreign Secretary]] from 2016 to 2018 and as [[Mayor of London]] from 2008 to 2016. Johnson has been [[Member of Parliament (United Kingdom)|Member of Parliament]] (MP) for [[Uxbridge and South Ruislip (UK Parliament constituency)|Uxbridge and South Ruislip]] since 2015, having previously been MP for [[Henley (UK Parliament constituency)|Henley]] from 2001 to 2008.\n\n<!--Early life, journalism, early political career, mayoralty and tenure as foreign secretary-->\nJohnson attended [[Eton College]], and studied [[Literae Humaniores|Classics]] at [[Balliol College, Oxford]]. He was elected [[List of presidents of the Oxford Union|president of the Oxford Union]] in 1986. In 1989, he became the Brussels correspondent \u2014 and later political columnist \u2014 for ''[[The Daily Telegraph]]'', and from 1999 to 2005 was the editor of ''[[The Spectator]]''. Following his election to parliament in 2001 he was a [[shadow minister]] under Conservative leaders [[Michael Howard]] and [[David Cameron]]. In 2008, Johnson [[2008 London mayoral election|was elected mayor of London]] and resigned from the House of Commons; he was [[2012 London mayoral election|re-elected mayor in 2012]]. At the [[2015 United Kingdom general election|2015 general election]] he was elected MP for [[Uxbridge]] and [[South Ruislip]], and the following year did not seek re-election as mayor. Johnson became a prominent figure in the successful [[Vote Leave]] campaign for [[Brexit]] in the [[2016 United Kingdom European Union membership referendum|2016 European Union (EU) membership referendum]]. [[Theresa May]] [[Boris Johnson's tenure as Foreign Secretary|appointed him foreign secretary]] after the referendum; he resigned the position two years later in protest at the [[Chequers Agreement]] and May's approach to Brexit.\n\n<!--Prime minister-->\nJohnson defeated [[Jeremy Hunt]] in the [[2019 Conservative Party leadership election|Conservative Party leadership election]] to succeed May, who resigned after [[Parliament of the United Kingdom|Parliament]]'s repeated rejections of her [[Brexit withdrawal agreement]]. After he was appointed prime minister, Johnson [[Brexit negotiations in 2019|re-opened Brexit negotiations]] and in early September [[2019 British prorogation controversy|controversially prorogued Parliament]]; the [[Supreme Court of the United Kingdom|Supreme Court]] later that month ruled the action unlawful.{{efn|name=prorogationtechnicality|Formally, Parliament is prorogued by the monarch (at the time Queen [[Elizabeth&nbsp;II]]) on the advice of the prime minister; it is a common [[legal fiction]] in the UK that many executive functions of the prime minister are formally carried out by the monarch on the prime minister's \"[[Advice (constitutional law)|advice]]\", which is effectively the legal instrument by which the prime minister carries out the function. It was this advice of Johnson's that was ruled unlawful, not the actions of the Queen.<ref>{{cite court |litigants=R (on the application of Miller) (Appellant) v The Prime Minister (Respondent) |pinpoint=12 |year=2019 |court=[[Supreme Court of the United Kingdom]] |url=https://www.supremecourt.uk/cases/docs/uksc-2019-0192-judgment.pdf}}</ref>}} After agreeing to a revised [[Brexit withdrawal agreement]], which replaced the [[Irish backstop]] with a new [[Northern Ireland Protocol]], but [[Parliamentary votes on Brexit|failing to win parliamentary support]] for the agreement, Johnson called a [[snap election]] for [[2019 United Kingdom general election|December 2019]] in which he led the [[Conservative Party (UK)|Conservative Party]] to victory with 43.6 per cent of the vote, and the party's largest seat share since [[1987 United Kingdom general election|the 1987 general election]]. On 31 January 2020, the United Kingdom withdrew from the EU, entering into a transition period and [[Trade negotiation between the UK and the EU|trade negotiations]] that led to the [[EU\u2013UK Trade and Cooperation Agreement]].\n\nA decisive event that shaped [[Premiership of Boris Johnson|Johnson's premiership]] was the [[COVID-19 pandemic in the United Kingdom|COVID-19 pandemic]]; [[British government response to the COVID-19 pandemic|the government responded]] by introducing various [[Coronavirus Act 2020|emergency powers]] and measures across society to mitigate the pandemic's impact, and approved the rollout of a [[COVID-19 vaccination in the United Kingdom|nationwide vaccination programme]]. He responded to the [[2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine]] by imposing [[International sanctions during the 2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine|sanctions on Russia]] and authorising [[List of foreign aid to Ukraine during the Russo-Ukrainian War|foreign aid and weapons shipments]] to Ukraine.<ref>{{Cite news |date=2022-06-29 |title=UK Gives \u00a31 Billion to Ukraine to Help Fund Offensive Operations |language=en |work=Bloomberg.com |url=https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2022-06-29/uk-gives-1-billion-to-ukraine-to-help-fund-offensive-operations |access-date=2022-07-07}}</ref> Amid the [[Partygate]] scandal, Johnson became the first prime minister to be sanctioned for breaking the law while in office after being issued a [[fixed penalty notice]] in April 2022 for breaching COVID-19 regulations during [[COVID-19 lockdown in the United Kingdom|lockdowns]]. The publishing of the [[Sue Gray report]] in May 2022 and a widespread sense of dissatisfaction led in June 2022 to a [[2022 vote of confidence in the Conservative Party leadership of Boris Johnson|vote of confidence]] in his leadership among Conservative MPs, which he won. In July 2022, revelations over his [[Chris Pincher scandal|appointment of Chris Pincher]] as Deputy Chief Whip led to a [[July 2022 United Kingdom government crisis|mass resignation of ministers from his government]] and to Johnson announcing his resignation. He left office on 6 September and was succeeded by [[Liz Truss]], his foreign secretary. Johnson remains in the House of Commons as a [[backbencher]].\n\n<!--Legacy and political positions-->\nJohnson is a [[Public image of Boris Johnson|controversial figure]] in British politics.<ref name=\"ABC\">{{cite news |last=Davies |first=Guy |date=23 July 2019 |title=Meet Boris Johnson: The UK's controversial new prime minister |website=[[ABC News]] 2021}}</ref><ref name=\"FT\">{{cite news |last=Blitz |first=James |date=23 July 2019 |title=Why is Boris Johnson such a divisive figure? |work=[[Financial Times]] |url=https://www.ft.com/content/bd03b736-ac7d-11e9-8030-530adfa879c2 |url-status=live |url-access=subscription |access-date=5 May 2021 and entertaining,{{sfn|Gimson|2012|p=20}} with an appeal stretching beyond traditional Conservative Party voters.<ref name=\"Telegraph150107\">{{cite news |last=Kirkup |first=James |date=7 January 2015 |title=Boris Johnson goes looking for Conservative friends in the north |website=The Telegraph |location=London |archive-url=https://ghostarchive.org/archive/20220110/https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/general-election-2015/11329569/Boris-Johnson-goes-looking-for-Conservative-friends-in-the-north.html |archive-date=10 January 2022}}{{cbignore}}</ref>{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=327}} Conversely, his critics have accused him of lying, [[elitism]], [[cronyism]] and [[bigotry]].{{sfn|Edwards|Isaby|2008|p=110}}<ref name=\"GuardChums\" />{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=365}} [[Political positions of Boris Johnson|Johnson's political positions]] have sometimes been described as following [[one-nation conservatism]], and commentators have characterised his political style as opportunistic, [[populist]], or pragmatic.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=121}}<ref name=\"IT190623\">{{cite news |last=Staunton |first=Denis |date=23 June 2019 |title=Boris Johnson: The UK's deeply polarising next prime minister |newspaper=[[The Irish Times]] |url=https://www.irishtimes.com/news/world/uk/boris-johnson-the-uk-s-deeply-polarising-next-prime-minister-1.3933181 |url-status=live |access-date=8 May 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210729062628/https://www.irishtimes.com/news/world/uk/boris-johnson-the-uk-s-deeply-polarising-next-prime-minister-1.3933181 |archive-date=29 July 2021}}</ref><ref>{{cite book |last=Berend |first=T. Iv\u00e1n |author-link=Iv\u00e1n T. Berend |title=A century of populist demagogues: Eighteen European portraits, 1918\u20132018 |publisher=[[Central European University Press]] |location=Budapest |date=2020 |page=239 |isbn=978-963-386-334-3 |jstor=10.7829/j.ctv16f6cn2.1}}</ref>\n\n{{TOC limit}}\n==Early life==\n===Childhood===\nAlexander Boris de Pfeffel Johnson was born on 19&nbsp;June 1964 in the [[Upper East Side]] of [[Manhattan]], [[New York City]],<ref>{{cite news |last=Lawless |first=Jill |date=23 July 2019 |title=Boris Johnson's Chaotic Path to Power Finally Pays Off |publisher=[[Associated Press]] |url=https://apnews.com/b48561749c65479fbadbcdc35808343a |access-date=31 July 2019 |archive-date=31 July 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190731085804/https://apnews.com/b48561749c65479fbadbcdc35808343a |url-status=live}}</ref>{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=10|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=1}} to 23-year-old [[Stanley Johnson (writer)|Stanley Johnson]], then studying economics at [[Columbia University]],{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=11|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=2}} and 22-year-old [[Charlotte Johnson Wahl|Charlotte Fawcett]],<ref name=\"telegraph.co.uk\">{{cite news |last=Llewellyn Smith |first=Julia |date=18 May 2008 |title=Boris Johnson, by his mother Charlotte Johnson Wahl |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/newstopics/borisjohnson/1976150/Boris-Johnson-by-his-mother-Charlotte-Johnson-Wahl.html |url-status=dead |work=[[The Sunday Telegraph]] |location=London |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110123154217/http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/newstopics/borisjohnson/1976150/Boris-Johnson-by-his-mother-Charlotte-Johnson-Wahl.html |archive-date=23 January 2011 |access-date=16 April 2020 |url-access=subscription}}</ref> an artist from a family of liberal intellectuals. Johnson's parents had married in 1963 before moving to the United States.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=11, 24\u201325|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=12\u201313}} They returned to their native England in September 1964, so that Charlotte could study at the [[University of Oxford]];{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=44|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2pp=12\u201313|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3p=11}} during this time she lived with her son in [[Summertown, Oxford|Summertown]], a suburb of [[Oxford]], and in 1965 she gave birth to a daughter, [[Rachel Johnson|Rachel]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=13}} In July 1965, the family moved to [[Crouch End]] in [[north London]],{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=14}} and in February 1966 they relocated to Washington, D.C., where Stanley had gained employment with the [[World Bank]].{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=44|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2p=16|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3p=14}} Stanley then took a job with a policy panel on [[population control]], and moved the family to [[Norwalk, Connecticut]], in June.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=16}} A third child, Leo, was born in September 1967.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=15|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=14}}\n\n[[File:Ashdown House - geograph.org.uk - 1691918.jpg|thumb|left|[[Ashdown House, East Sussex|Ashdown House]] preparatory school, East Sussex, attended by Johnson from 1975 to 1977]]\n\nIn 1969, the family returned to England and settled into West Nethercote Farm, near [[Winsford, Somerset|Winsford]] in Somerset, Stanley's remote family home on [[Exmoor]] in the [[West Country]].{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=44|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2p=17|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3p=17, 20\u201322}} There, Johnson gained his first experiences of [[fox hunting]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=18}} His father was regularly absent from Nethercote, leaving Johnson to be raised largely by his mother, assisted by [[au pair]]s.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=18|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=25}} As a child, Johnson was quiet and studious{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=14}} and was deaf, resulting in several operations to insert [[Tympanostomy tube|grommets]] into his ears.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=13|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=11}} He and his siblings were encouraged to engage in [[highbrow]] activities from a young age,{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=26|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=18}} with high achievement being greatly valued; Johnson's earliest recorded ambition was to be \"world king\".{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=45|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2p=28|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3pp=17\u201318}} Having few or no friends other than their siblings, the children became very close.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=28\u201329}}\n\nIn late 1969, the family moved to [[Maida Vale]] in west London, while Stanley began [[Postgraduate education|post-graduate]] research at the [[London School of Economics]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=29}} In 1970, Charlotte and the children briefly returned to Nethercote, where Johnson attended Winsford Village School, before returning to London to settle in [[Primrose Hill]],{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=30}} where they were educated at Primrose Hill Primary School.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=44|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2p=30|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3p=26}} A fourth child and third son, [[Jo Johnson|Joseph]], was born in late 1971.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=31}}\n\nAfter Stanley secured employment at the [[European Commission]], he moved his family in April 1973 to [[Uccle]], Brussels, where Johnson attended the [[European School, Brussels&nbsp;I]] and learnt to speak French.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=44|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2pp=31\u201332|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3p=26}}<ref>{{cite news |last=Johnson |first=Stanley |author-link=Stanley Johnson (writer) |title=Stanley Johnson: Why I remain a fan of Brussels |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/travel/destinations/europe/belgium/brussels/articles/stanley-johnson-why-i-remain-a-fan-of-brussels/ |url-status=live |url-access=limited |work=[[The Daily Telegraph]] |location=London |date=18 June 2016 |access-date=5 May 2021 |archive-url=https://archive.today/2016.07.01-222600/http://www.telegraph.co.uk/travel/destinations/europe/belgium/brussels/articles/stanley-johnson-why-i-remain-a-fan-of-brussels/ |archive-date=1 July 2016}}</ref> Charlotte had a [[nervous breakdown]] and was hospitalised with [[Major depressive disorder|clinical depression]], after which Johnson and his siblings were sent back to England in 1975 to attend [[Ashdown House, East Sussex|Ashdown House]], a preparatory boarding school in East Sussex.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=44|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2pp=33\u201335|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3pp=27\u201329}} There, he developed a love of [[Rugby football|rugby]] and excelled at [[Ancient Greek]] and [[Latin]],{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=42|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=30\u201331}} but the teachers' use of [[school corporal punishment|corporal punishment]] appalled him.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=41|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=33}} Meanwhile, in December 1978 his parents' relationship broke down; they divorced in 1980,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=36, 42}} and Charlotte moved into a flat in [[Notting Hill]], west London, where her children joined her for much of their time.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1pp=44\u201345|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2pp=38\u201339|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3p=35}}\n\n===Eton and Oxford: 1977\u20131987===\n{{quote box|width=25em|align=right|quote=As a kid I was extremely spotty, extremely nerdy and horribly {{wikteng|swotty}}. My idea of a really good time was to travel across London on the tube to visit the [[British Museum]].|author= \u2014Boris Johnson{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=49}} }}\n\n[[File:Oxford - Balliol College - geograph.org.uk - 1329613.jpg|thumb|right|Johnson read Classics at [[Balliol College, Oxford]].]]\n\nJohnson gained a [[King's Scholar]]ship to study at [[Eton College]], a boarding school near [[Windsor, Berkshire|Windsor]] in [[Berkshire]].{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=44|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2p=42}} Arriving in the autumn term of 1977,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=45}} he began using his middle name Boris rather than his first name Alexander,<ref>{{cite web|title=The 10 ages of Boris Johnson: a guide to his road to power|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/jul/21/boris-johnson-route-to-number-10|work=[[The Guardian]]|last=Sonia|first=Purnell|date=21 July 2019|access-date=30 July 2022|archive-date=11 July 2022|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220711204147/https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/jul/21/boris-johnson-route-to-number-10|url-status=live}}</ref> and developed \"the eccentric English persona\" for which he became famous.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=47\u201348}} He abandoned his mother's Catholicism and became an [[Anglicanism|Anglican]], joining the [[Church of England]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=48}} School reports complained about his idleness, complacency and lateness,{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=44|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2pp=50\u201351|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3pp=41\u201344}} but he was popular and well known at Eton.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=47\u201348}} His friends were largely from the wealthy upper-middle and upper classes, his best friends then being [[Darius Guppy]] and [[Charles Spencer, 9th Earl Spencer|Charles Spencer]], both of whom later accompanied him to the [[University of Oxford]] and remained friends into adulthood.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=53}} Johnson excelled in [[English studies|English]] and the [[Classics]], winning prizes in both,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=49\u201350}} and became secretary of the school [[debating society]],{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=55}} and editor of the school newspaper, ''The Eton College Chronicle''.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=49, 53}} In late 1981, he was elected a member of [[Eton Society|''Pop'']],{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=54\u201355|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=51\u201352}} the small, self-selecting elite and glamorous group of prefects. Later in Johnson's career it was a point of rivalry with [[David Cameron]], who had failed to enter ''Pop''. On leaving Eton, Johnson went on a [[gap year]] to Australia, where he taught English and Latin at [[Timbertop (Geelong Grammar School)|Timbertop]], an [[Outward Bound]]-inspired campus of [[Geelong Grammar School|Geelong Grammar]], an elite independent boarding school.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=58\u201359}}<ref>{{Cite news |last=Bunbury |first=Stephanie |date=17 August 2013 |title=Boris Johnson and the right to write |work=[[The Sydney Morning Herald]] |url=https://www.smh.com.au/entertainment/books/boris-johnson-and-the-right-to-write-20130815-2ryy9.html |access-date=12 January 2018 |archive-date=13 January 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180113041055/http://www.smh.com.au/entertainment/books/boris-johnson-and-the-right-to-write-20130815-2ryy9.html |url-status=live }}</ref><ref>{{Cite news |last=Johnston |first=Chris |date=15 July 2016 |title=When Boris was just another Timbertop Rent-A-Pom |work=[[The Border Mail]] |url=http://www.bordermail.com.au/story/4033888/when-boris-was-just-another-timbertop-rent-a-pom/?cs=4067 |access-date=12 January 2018 |archive-date=13 January 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180113035552/http://www.bordermail.com.au/story/4033888/when-boris-was-just-another-timbertop-rent-a-pom/?cs=4067 |url-status=live }}</ref>\n\nJohnson won a scholarship to read ''[[Literae Humaniores]]'' at [[Balliol College, Oxford]], a four-year course in the study of the Classics, ancient literature and classical philosophy.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=45|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2p=57|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3p=83}} [[Matriculating]] at the university in late 1983,{{sfn|Gimson|2012|p=56}} he was one of a generation of Oxford undergraduates who were later to dominate British politics and media in the second decade of the 21st century; among them David Cameron, [[William Hague]], [[Michael Gove]], [[Jeremy Hunt]] and [[Nick Boles]], who all went on to become senior Conservative Party politicians.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=62}} While at Oxford, Johnson participated in college [[rugby union]], playing as a [[Prop (rugby union)|tighthead prop]] for [[Balliol College, Oxford|Balliol College]]'s team for four years.<ref>{{Cite book |last=Johnson |first=Boris |title=Friends, Voters, Countrymen: Jottings on the Stump |publisher=[[HarperCollins]] |year=2001 |isbn=0007119135 |location=London |pages=151}}</ref> To his later regret, he joined the Old Etonian-dominated [[Bullingdon Club]], an exclusive drinking society notorious for acts of vandalism on host premises.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=63\u201365|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=63\u201366}}<ref>{{Cite news |date=28 May 2013 |title=David Dimbleby Slams 'Disgraceful' Boris Johnson For Ruining Bullingdon Club |work=[[HuffPost]] |url=http://www.huffingtonpost.co.uk/2013/05/28/david-dimbleby-bullingdon-club-boris-johnson_n_3345077.html |access-date=29 May 2014 |url-status=live |archive-date=11 December 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20181211065921/https://www.huffingtonpost.co.uk/2013/05/28/david-dimbleby-bullingdon-club-boris-johnson_n_3345077.html }}</ref><ref>{{Cite news |last=Kingsley |first=Patrick |date=10 August 2011 |title=UK riots: how do Boris Johnson's Bullingdon antics compare? |work=[[The Guardian]] |location=London |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2011/aug/10/uk-riots-boris-johnson |access-date=29 May 2014 |url-status=live |archive-date=6 February 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210206061641/https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2011/aug/10/uk-riots-boris-johnson }}</ref> Many years later, a group photograph including himself and Cameron in Bullingdon Club formal dress led to much negative press coverage. He began a relationship with [[Allegra Mostyn-Owen]], cover girl for ''[[Tatler]]'' magazine and daughter of [[Christie's Education]] chairman [[William Mostyn-Owen]]. She was a glamorous and popular fellow student from his own social background; they became engaged while at university.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=72, 74\u201378|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=76\u201383}}\n\nJohnson was popular and well known at Oxford.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=70|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=60}} Alongside Guppy, he co-edited the university's satirical magazine ''Tributary''.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=68|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=74}} In 1984, Johnson was elected secretary of the [[Oxford Union]],{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=70\u201371}} and campaigned unsuccessfully for the career-enhancing and important position of [[List of Presidents of the Oxford Union|Union President]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=71\u201373}} In 1986, Johnson ran successfully for president,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=80\u201381}} but his term was not particularly distinguished or memorable{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=84}} and questions were raised regarding his competence and seriousness.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=87}} Finally, Johnson was awarded an [[Upper second-class honours|upper second-class]] degree,{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=89\u201390|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=84}}<ref>{{Cite news |last=Barber |first=Lyn |date=5 October 2003 |title=Charmed, I'm Sure |work=The Guardian |location=London |url=https://www.theguardian.com/media/2003/oct/05/pressandpublishing.politicsphilosophyandsociety |access-date=8 April 2020 |archive-date=6 February 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210206061733/https://www.theguardian.com/media/2003/oct/05/pressandpublishing.politicsphilosophyandsociety |url-status=live }}</ref> and was deeply unhappy he did not receive a first.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=92}}\n\n==Early career==\n===''The Times'' and ''The Daily Telegraph'': 1987\u20131994===\n{{quote box|width=25em|align=right|quote=I saw the whole [European Union] change. It was a wonderful time to be there. The [[Berlin Wall]] fell and the French and Germans had to decide how they were going to respond to this event, and what was Europe going to become, and there was this fantastic pressure to create a single polity, to create an answer to the historic German problem, and this produced the most fantastic strains in the Conservative Party, so everything I wrote from Brussels, I found was sort of chucking these rocks over the garden wall and I listened to this amazing crash from the greenhouse next door over in England as everything I wrote from Brussels was having this amazing, explosive effect on the Tory party, and it really gave me this I suppose rather weird sense of power.|author= \u2013Boris Johnson{{sfn|Gimson|2012|p=102}}}}\n\nIn September 1987, Johnson and Mostyn-Owen were married in [[West Felton]], Shropshire, accompanied by a duet for violin and viola ''Allegra e Boris''<ref>{{Cite web|url=https://en.schott-music.com/shop/allegra-e-boris-no272687.html|title=Allegra e Boris|website=englisch|access-date=30 July 2022|archive-date=23 June 2019|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190623104052/https://en.schott-music.com/shop/allegra-e-boris-no272687.html|url-status=live}}</ref> specially commissioned for the wedding from [[Hans Werner Henze]].{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=92\u201394|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=85\u201386}} After a honeymoon in Egypt, they settled in [[West Kensington]], west London,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=94}} when he secured work for a [[management consultancy]] company, [[L.E.K. Consulting]]; he resigned after a week.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=46|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2pp=94\u201395|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3pp=87\u201388}} In late 1987, through family connections, he began work as a graduate trainee at ''[[The Times]]''.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=95\u201399|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=88\u201390}} Scandal erupted when Johnson wrote an article for the newspaper, on the [[archaeological]] discovery of [[Edward II of England|Edward\u00a0II]]'s palace, having invented a quote for the article which he falsely attributed to the historian [[Colin Lucas]], his godfather. After the editor [[Charles Wilson (journalist)|Charles Wilson]] learnt of the matter, he dismissed Johnson.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=100\u2013102|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=90\u201396}}\n\nJohnson secured employment on the leader-writing desk of ''[[The Daily Telegraph]]'', having met its editor, [[Max Hastings]], during his Oxford University Union presidency.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=102\u2013103|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=97}} His articles appealed to the newspaper's conservative, middle-class, middle-aged \"[[Middle England]]\" readership,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=108}} and were known for their distinctive literary style, replete with old-fashioned words and phrases and for regularly referring to the readership as \"my friends\".{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=106\u2013107}} In early 1989, Johnson was appointed to the newspaper's Brussels bureau to report on the European Commission,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=109}} remaining in the post until 1994.<ref name=\"Fletcher\">{{cite news |last=Fletcher |first=Martin |date=4 November 2017 |title=The joke's over \u2013 how Boris Johnson is damaging Britain's global stature |work=[[New Statesman]] |url=https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/uk/2017/11/joke-s-over-how-boris-johnson-damaging-britain-s-global-stature |access-date=7 November 2017 |archive-date=6 February 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210206063009/https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/uk/2017/11/joke-s-over-how-boris-johnson-damaging-britain-s-global-stature |url-status=live}}</ref> A strong critic of the integrationist Commission President [[Jacques Delors]], he established himself as one of the city's few [[Eurosceptic]] journalists.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=115\u2013116}} He wrote articles about [[euromyths]] such as the EU wanting to ban [[prawn cocktail]] crisps and British sausages, and to standardise condom sizes because Italians had smaller penises.<ref>{{cite news |last=Mount |first=Ferdinand |author-link=Ferdinand Mount |title=Ruthless and Truthless |url=https://www.lrb.co.uk/the-paper/v43/n09/ferdinand-mount/ruthless-and-truthless |access-date=17 June 2021 |work=[[London Review of Books]] |date=6 May 2021 |archive-date=8 June 2022 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220608071851/https://www.lrb.co.uk/the-paper/v43/n09/ferdinand-mount/ruthless-and-truthless |url-status=live}}</ref> He wrote that Brussels had recruited sniffers to ensure that Euro-manure smells the same,{{sfn|Bower|2021|pp=68, 627}} and that the Eurocrats were about to dictate the acceptable curve of bananas{{efn|[[Commission Regulation (EC) No. 2257/94]] introduced the requirement for bananas to be \"free from malformation or abnormal curvature\"; different standards applied to different classes of banana.<ref>{{cite news |last=Henley |first=Jon |title=Is the EU really dictating the shape of your bananas? |work=The Guardian |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2016/may/11/boris-johnson-launches-the-vote-leave-battlebus-in-cornwall |date=11 May 2016 |access-date=28 November 2021 |archive-date=18 May 2022 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220518134152/https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2016/may/11/boris-johnson-launches-the-vote-leave-battlebus-in-cornwall |url-status=live}}</ref>}} and the limits on the power of vacuum cleaners,<ref>{{cite news |last=Henley |first=Jon |title=Is the EU really dictating the shape of your bananas? |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2016/may/11/boris-johnson-launches-the-vote-leave-battlebus-in-cornwall |url-status=live |work=[[The Guardian]] |date=11 May 2016 |access-date=8 November 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220518134152/https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2016/may/11/boris-johnson-launches-the-vote-leave-battlebus-in-cornwall |archive-date=18 May 2022}}</ref>{{efn|The EU introduced limits on the power of vacuum cleaners in 2014.<ref>{{cite news |last=Smithers |first=Rebecca |date=21 August 2014 |title=EU ban on powerful vacuum cleaners prompts anger and legal challenge |work=The Guardian |url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2014/aug/21/anger-as-eu-bans-most-powerful-vacuum-cleaners |access-date=28 November 2021 |archive-date=28 November 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20211128200311/https://www.theguardian.com/world/2014/aug/21/anger-as-eu-bans-most-powerful-vacuum-cleaners |url-status=live}}</ref><ref>{{cite web |last=Dallison |first=Paul |date=23 February 2016 |title=Boris Johnson's 11 best Europe moments |url=https://www.politico.eu/article/the-eu-is-a-lobster-boris-johnson-best-moments-europe-reporting/ |access-date=28 November 2021 |website=Politico Europe |archive-date=28 November 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20211128200309/https://www.politico.eu/article/the-eu-is-a-lobster-boris-johnson-best-moments-europe-reporting/ |url-status=live}}</ref>}} and to order women to return their old sex toys.{{sfn|Bower|2021|pp=68, 627}} He wrote that euro notes made people impotent, that euro coins made people sick, and that a plan to blow up the [[Berlaymont building]] was in place because asbestos cladding made the building too dangerous to inhabit.{{sfn|Bower|2021|pp=68, 627}} Many of his fellow journalists there were critical of his articles, opining they often contained lies designed to discredit the commission.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=121, 126|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=98\u201399, 100\u2013101}} The Europhile Conservative politician [[Chris Patten]] later stated at that time Johnson was \"one of the greatest exponents of fake journalism\".<ref name=\"Fletcher\" /> Johnson opposed banning handguns after the [[Dunblane school massacre]], writing in his column \"Nanny is confiscating their toys. It is like one of those vast [[Compulsory sterilization#India|Indian programmes of compulsory vasectomy]].\"<ref>{{cite web|date=25 March 2021|title=Boris Johnson handgun quote is real|url=https://fullfact.org/online/boris-johnson-dunblane-guns/|access-date=10 October 2021|website=Full Fact|archive-date=8 June 2022|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220608071851/https://fullfact.org/online/boris-johnson-dunblane-guns/|url-status=live}}</ref>\n\nJohnson biographer [[Andrew Gimson]] believed these articles made him \"one of [Euroscepticism's] most famous exponents\".{{sfn|Gimson|2012|p=102}} According to later biographer [[Sonia Purnell|Sonia Purnell{{emdash}}]]<nowiki/>who was Johnson's Brussels deputy<ref name=\"Fletcher\" />{{emdash}}he helped make Euroscepticism \"an attractive and emotionally resonant cause for the Right\", whereas it had been associated previously with the British Left.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=115}} Johnson's articles established him as the favourite journalist of the Conservative Prime Minister [[Margaret Thatcher]],{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=120|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=104}} but Johnson annoyed her successor, the Europhile [[John Major]], who spent a great deal of time attempting to refute what Johnson said.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=118, 124}} Johnson's articles exacerbated tensions between the Conservative Party's Eurosceptic and Europhile factions. As a result, he earned the mistrust of many party members.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=124}} His writings were also a key influence on the emergence of the EU-opposing [[UK Independence Party]] (UKIP) in the early 1990s.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=115}} [[Conrad Black]], then proprietor of ''The Daily Telegraph'', said Johnson \"was such an effective correspondent for us in Brussels that he greatly influenced British opinion on this country's relations with Europe\".<ref>{{Cite web |last=Black |first=Conrad |date=29 June 2019 |title=Max Hastings vs Boris Johnson: I know who I'd trust more |url=https://www.spectator.co.uk/article/battle-of-hastings |access-date=9 April 2020 |website=[[The Spectator]] |archive-date=31 March 2022 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220331165808/https://www.spectator.co.uk/article/battle-of-hastings |url-status=live }}</ref>\n\nIn February 1990, Johnson's wife Allegra left him; after several attempts at reconciliation, their marriage ended in April 1993.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=129\u2013130, 134|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=107\u2013111, 113\u2013114}}<ref name=guardian200218/> He then entered a relationship with a childhood friend, [[Marina Wheeler]], who had moved to Brussels in 1990,{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=130\u2013133|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=111\u2013114}} and in May 1993, they were married at [[Horsham]] in Sussex,{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=134|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=114\u2013115}} soon after which Marina gave birth to a daughter.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=135|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=115}} Johnson and his new wife settled in [[Islington]], north London,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=142}} an area known for its association with the left-liberal [[intelligentsia]]. Under the influence of this ''milieu'' and of his wife, Johnson moved in a more liberal direction on issues like [[climate change]], [[LGBT rights]] and race relations.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=144, 148}} While in Islington, the couple had three more children, all given the surname Johnson-Wheeler.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=143|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=125}} They were sent to the local Canonbury Primary School and then to private secondary schools.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=146\u2013147}} Devoting much time to his children, Johnson wrote a book of verse, ''Perils of the Pushy Parents&nbsp;\u2013 A Cautionary Tale'', which was published to largely poor reviews.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=153}}\n\n===Political columnist: 1994\u20131999===\nBack in London, Hastings turned down Johnson's request to become a [[war reporter]],{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=139\u2013140}} instead promoting him to the position of assistant editor and chief political columnist.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=161|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=124}} Johnson's column received praise for being ideologically eclectic and distinctively written, and earned him a Commentator of the Year Award at the ''[[What the Papers Say]]'' awards.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=168|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=125}} Some critics condemned his writing style as bigotry; in various columns he used the words \"[[piccaninnies]]\" and \"[[Watermelon stereotype|watermelon smiles]]\" when referring to Africans, championed European colonialism in Uganda{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=169}}<ref>{{cite news |last=Johnson |first=Boris |date=10 January 2002 |title=If Blair's so good at running the Congo, let him stay there |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/politics/0/blairs-good-running-congo-let-stay/ |url-status=live |work=The Daily Telegraph |archive-url=https://archive.today/2020.11.08-122710/https://www.telegraph.co.uk/politics/0/blairs-good-running-congo-let-stay/ |archive-date=8 November 2020 |access-date=5 May 2021 |url-access=limited}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |last=Johnson |first=Boris |date=1 September 2005 |title=Getting our knickers in a twist over China |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/comment/personal-view/3619424/Getting-our-knickers-in-a-twist-over-China.html |url-status=live |work=The Daily Telegraph |archive-url=https://archive.today/2020.03.05-160557/https://www.telegraph.co.uk/comment/personal-view/3619424/Getting-our-knickers-in-a-twist-over-China.html |archive-date=5 March 2020 |access-date=5 May 2021 |url-access=limited}}</ref> and referred to gay men as \"tank-topped bumboys\".{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=171}}\n\nContemplating a political career, in 1993, Johnson outlined his desire to stand as a Conservative candidate to be a [[Member of the European Parliament]] (MEP) in the [[1994 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom|1994 European Parliament elections]]. [[Andrew Mitchell]] convinced Major not to veto Johnson's candidacy, but Johnson could not find a constituency.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=162\u2013165}} He subsequently turned his attention to obtaining a seat in the UK House of Commons. After being rejected as Conservative candidate for [[Holborn and St Pancras (UK Parliament constituency)|Holborn and St. Pancras]], his party selected him the candidate for [[Clwyd South (UK Parliament constituency)|Clwyd South]] in north Wales, then a [[Labour Party (UK)|Labour Party]] [[safe seat]]. Spending six weeks campaigning, he attained 9,091 votes (23 per cent) in the [[1997 United Kingdom general election|1997 general election]], losing to the Labour candidate.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=46|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2pp=185\u2013186|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3p=125}}\n\nScandal erupted in June 1995 when a recording of a 1990 telephone conversation between Johnson and his friend [[Darius Guppy]] was made public.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=173\u2013176|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=117\u2013123}} In the conversation, Guppy said that his criminal activities involving insurance fraud were being investigated by ''[[News of the World]]'' journalist Stuart Collier, and he asked Johnson to provide him with Collier's private address, seeking to have the latter beaten to the extent of \"a couple of black eyes and a cracked rib or something like that\". Johnson agreed to supply the information, although he expressed concern that he would be associated with the attack.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=173\u2013176|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=117\u2013123}} When the phone conversation was published in 1995, Johnson stated that ultimately he had not obliged Guppy's request. Hastings reprimanded Johnson but did not dismiss him.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=173\u2013176|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=117\u2013123}}\n\nJohnson was given a regular column in ''[[The Spectator]]'', sister publication to ''The Daily Telegraph'', which attracted mixed reviews and was often thought rushed.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=168}} In 1999, he was also given a column reviewing new cars in the magazine ''[[GQ]]''.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=171|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=177\u2013178}} His behaviour regularly disgruntled his editors; the large number of parking fines that Johnson acquired while testing cars frustrated ''GQ'' staff.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=171}} At ''The Daily Telegraph'' and ''The Spectator'', he was consistently late delivering his copy, forcing many staff to stay late to accommodate him; some related that if they published without his work included, he would get angry and shout at them with expletives.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=179\u2013181|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=177}}\n\nJohnson's appearance on an April 1998 episode of the BBC's satirical current affairs show ''[[Have I Got News for You]]'' brought him national fame.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2019/10/29/arts/television/have-i-got-news-for-you-boris-johnson-brexit.html |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20191029104004/https://www.nytimes.com/2019/10/29/arts/television/have-i-got-news-for-you-boris-johnson-brexit.html |archive-date=29 October 2019 |url-access=subscription |url-status=live |title=A Show That Laughs at Boris Johnson, and May Have Aided His Rise|work=[[The New York Times]]|date=29 October 2019 |access-date=10 June 2021|last1=Marshall |first1=Alex }}</ref> He was invited back on to later episodes, including as a guest presenter; for his 2003 appearance, Johnson received a nomination for the [[British Academy Television Award for Best Entertainment Performance|BAFTA Television Award for Best Entertainment Performance]].{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=176\u2013178|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=127\u2013129}}<ref>{{Cite web |date=18 April 2020 |title=Television in 2004 \u2013 BAFTA Awards |url=http://awards.bafta.org/award/2004/television |access-date=3 May 2020 |website=[[BAFTA]] |archive-date=14 August 2017 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20170814063714/http://awards.bafta.org/award/2004/television |url-status=live }}</ref> After these appearances, he came to be recognised on the street by the public, and was invited to appear on other television shows, such as ''[[Top Gear (2002 TV series)|Top Gear]]'', ''[[Parkinson (TV series)|Parkinson]]'', ''[[Breakfast with Frost]]'', and the political show ''[[Question Time (TV programme)|Question Time]]''.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=178|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=130}}\n\n===''The Spectator'' and MP for Henley: 1999\u20132008===\nIn July 1999, Conrad Black offered Johnson the editorship of ''The Spectator'' on the condition he abandon his parliamentary aspirations; Johnson agreed.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=188|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=131}} While retaining ''The Spectator''{{'}}s traditional right-wing bent, Johnson welcomed contributions from leftist writers and cartoonists.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=198\u2013199}} Under Johnson's editorship, the magazine's circulation grew by 10% to 62,000 and it began to turn a profit.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=191}} His editorship also drew criticism; some opined that under him ''The Spectator'' avoided serious issues,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=204}} while colleagues became annoyed that he was regularly absent from the office, meetings, and events.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=192, 194}} He gained a reputation as a poor political pundit because of incorrect political predictions made in the magazine.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=204}} His father-in-law <!--Not knighted until the 2006 birthday honours-->[[Charles Wheeler (journalist)|Charles Wheeler]] and others strongly criticised him for allowing ''Spectator'' columnist [[Taki Theodoracopulos]] to publish racist and antisemitic language in the magazine.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=193}}<ref>{{Cite news |date=2 April 2008 |title=Boris says sorry over 'blacks have lower IQs' article in the Spectator |work=[[Evening Standard]] |url=https://www.standard.co.uk/news/mayor/boris-says-sorry-over-blacks-have-lower-iqs-article-in-the-spectator-6630340.html |access-date=30 August 2018 |archive-date=31 August 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180831010906/https://www.standard.co.uk/news/mayor/boris-says-sorry-over-blacks-have-lower-iqs-article-in-the-spectator-6630340.html |url-status=live }}</ref>\n\nJournalist Charlotte Edwardes wrote in ''The Times'' in 2019 alleging that Johnson had squeezed her thigh at a private lunch in the offices of the ''Spectator'' in 1999 and that another woman had told her he had done the same to her. A Downing Street spokesman denied the allegation.<ref>{{Cite news |date=29 September 2019 |title=No 10 denies Boris Johnson 'thigh squeeze' claim |work=[[BBC News]] |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49873301 |access-date=29 September 2019 |archive-date=29 September 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190929190514/https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49873301 |url-status=live }}</ref><ref>{{Cite web|last=Edwardes|first=Charlotte|date=29 September 2019|title=Charlotte Edwardes on Boris Johnson's wandering hands|url=https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/charlotte-edwardes-on-boris-johnson-s-wandering-hands-hnxnqwbb8|url-access=subscription|access-date=7 August 2021|website=[[The Times]]|archive-date=7 August 2021|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210807164700/https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/charlotte-edwardes-on-boris-johnson-s-wandering-hands-hnxnqwbb8|url-status=live}}</ref>\n\nIn 2004, Johnson published an editorial in ''The Spectator'' after the murder of [[Ken Bigley]] suggesting that Liverpudlians were wallowing in their victim status and also \"hooked on grief\" over the [[Hillsborough disaster]], which Johnson partly blamed on \"drunken fans\".<ref>{{Cite news |last=Pidd |first=Helen |date=11 August 2019 |title=PM should apologise for Hillsborough remarks, says Liverpool mayor |work=The Guardian |url=https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2019/aug/11/boris-johnson-should-apologise-for-spectator-hillsborough-remarks-says-liverpool-mayor |access-date=30 July 2022 |archive-date=17 July 2022 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220717173734/https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2019/aug/11/boris-johnson-should-apologise-for-spectator-hillsborough-remarks-says-liverpool-mayor |url-status=live }}</ref><ref>{{cite news |title=Boris sorry for Scouse stereotype |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/uk_politics/3756418.stm |access-date=27 June 2020 |work=BBC News |date=19 October 2004 |archive-date=22 May 2006 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20060522110858/http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/uk_politics/3756418.stm |url-status=live }}</ref> In an appendix added to a later edition of his 2005 book about the Roman empire, ''[[The Dream of Rome]]'', [[Tell MAMA]] and the [[Muslim Council of Britain]] strongly criticised Johnson for arguing [[Islam]] has caused the [[Muslim world]] to be \"literally centuries behind\" [[Western world|the West]].<ref name=\"theguardian\">{{Cite news |last=Perraudin |first=Frances |date=15 July 2019 |title=Boris Johnson claimed Islam put Muslim world 'centuries behind' |work=The Guardian |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/jul/15/boris-johnson-islam-muslim-world-centuries-behind-2007-essay |access-date=27 July 2019 |archive-date=27 July 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190727063050/https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/jul/15/boris-johnson-islam-muslim-world-centuries-behind-2007-essay |url-status=live }}</ref>\n\n====Becoming an MP====\n{{quote box|width=25em|align=right|quote=The selection of Boris Johnson&nbsp;... confirms the Tory Party's increasing weakness for celebrity personalities over the dreary exigencies of politics. Johnson, for all his gifts, is unlikely to grace any future Tory cabinet. Indeed, he is not known for his excessive interest in serious policy matters, and it is hard to see him grubbing away at administrative detail as an obscure, hardworking junior minister for social security. To maintain his funny man reputation he will no doubt find himself refining his [[Bertie Wooster]] interpretation to the point where the impersonation becomes the man.|author= \u2013Max Hastings, ''London Evening Standard''|source={{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=219}} }}\n\nFollowing [[Michael Heseltine]]'s retirement, Johnson decided to stand as Conservative candidate for [[Henley (UK Parliament constituency)|Henley]], a Conservative safe seat in [[Oxfordshire]].{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=209\u2013210|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=141}} The local Conservative branch selected him although it was split over Johnson's candidacy. Some thought him amusing and charming while others disliked his flippant attitude and lack of knowledge of the local area.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=211\u2013212}} Boosted by his television fame, Johnson won the seat in the [[2001 United Kingdom general election|2001 general election]] with a majority of 8,500 votes.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=220\u2013221}} Alongside his Islington home, Johnson bought a farmhouse outside [[Thame]] in his new constituency.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=240}} He regularly attended Henley social events and occasionally wrote for the ''[[Henley Standard]]''.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=238}} His constituency surgeries proved popular, and he joined local campaigns to stop the closure of [[Royal Berkshire NHS Foundation Trust|Townlands Hospital]] and the local [[Thames Valley Air Ambulance|air ambulance]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=239}}\n\nIn Parliament, Johnson was appointed to a [[standing committee]] assessing the [[Proceeds of Crime Act 2002|Proceeds of Crime Bill]], but missed many of its meetings.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=225}} Despite his credentials as a public speaker, his speeches in the House of Commons were widely deemed lacklustre; Johnson later called them \"crap\".{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=232\u2013233}} In his first four years as MP, he attended just over half of the Commons votes; in his second term, this declined to 45 per cent.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=230}} He usually supported the Conservative [[party line (politics)|party line]] but rebelled against it five times in this period.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=231}} In [[free votes]], he demonstrated a more socially liberal attitude than many colleagues, supporting the [[Gender Recognition Act 2004]] and the repeal of [[Section 28]].<ref>{{cite news|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/uk_politics/2664163.stm|title=Section 28 compromise avoids a crisis|date=16 January 2003|work=BBC News|access-date=30 July 2022|archive-date=27 May 2006|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20060527114531/http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/uk_politics/2664163.stm|url-status=live}}</ref><ref name=\"Conservative Revolts\">{{cite web|url=http://www.revolts.co.uk/Conservative%20splits.pdf |title=Mapping Conservative Divisions Under Michael Howard |access-date=3 February 2010 |last1=Cowley |first1=Philip |last2=Stuart |first2=Mark |date=19 November 2004 |publisher=Revolts.co.uk |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110606033124/http://www.revolts.co.uk/Conservative%20splits.pdf |archive-date=6 June 2011 |url-status=dead}}</ref> However, in 2001, Johnson had spoken out against plans to repeal Section 28, saying it was \"Labour's appalling agenda, encouraging the teaching of homosexuality in schools\".<ref>{{cite news |last=Johnson |first=Boris |title=Another voice |url=http://archive.spectator.co.uk/article/15th-april-2000/8/another-voice |access-date=6 June 2021 |work=[[The Spectator]] |date=15 April 2000 |page=8 |archive-date=19 May 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210519015210/http://archive.spectator.co.uk/article/15th-april-2000/8/another-voice |url-status=live}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |last=Cooper |first=Alex |title=Boris Johnson gets mixed reviews from U.K.'s LGBTQ community |url=https://www.nbcnews.com/feature/nbc-out/boris-johnson-gets-mixed-reviews-u-k-s-lgbtq-community-n1035241 |access-date=6 June 2021 |publisher=[[NBC News]] |date=26 July 2019 |archive-date=6 June 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210606212010/https://www.nbcnews.com/feature/nbc-out/boris-johnson-gets-mixed-reviews-u-k-s-lgbtq-community-n1035241 |url-status=live}}</ref> After initially stating he would not, he [[British Parliamentary approval for the invasion of Iraq|voted in support]] of the government's plans to join the US in the [[2003 invasion of Iraq]],{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=240}} and in April 2003 visited occupied Baghdad.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=149}} In August 2004, he backed unsuccessful [[Parliamentary motion to impeach Tony Blair|impeachment procedures]] against Prime Minister [[Tony Blair]] for \"[[high crimes and misdemeanours]]\" regarding the war,<ref>{{cite news |date=2 September 2004 |title=Programmes = BBC Parliament \u2013 Impeachment in practice |publisher=BBC |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/programmes/bbc_parliament/3622380.stm |access-date=18 December 2016 |archive-date=16 November 2020 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20201116220243/http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/programmes/bbc_parliament/3622380.stm |url-status=live}}</ref> and in December 2006 described the invasion as \"a colossal mistake and misadventure\".{{sfn|Gimson|2012|p=265}}\n\nAlthough labelling Johnson \"ineffably duplicitous\" for breaking his promise not to become an MP, Black decided not to dismiss him because he \"helped promote the magazine and raise its circulation\".{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=189, 190}} Johnson remained editor of ''The Spectator'', also writing columns for ''The Daily Telegraph'' and ''GQ'', and making television appearances.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=226}} His 2001 book, ''[[Friends, Voters, Countrymen|Friends, Voters, Countrymen: Jottings on the Stump]]'', recounted that year's election campaign,{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=230|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=145\u2013146}} while 2003's ''Lend Me Your Ears'' collected together previously published columns and articles.{{sfn|Gimson|2012|pp=176\u2013177}} In 2004, [[HarperCollins]] published his first novel: ''[[Seventy-Two Virgins|Seventy-Two Virgins: A Comedy of Errors]]'' revolved around the life of a Conservative MP and contained various autobiographical elements.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=249\u2013251|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=189\u2013192}} Responding to critics who argued he was juggling too many jobs, he cited [[Winston Churchill]] and [[Benjamin Disraeli]] as exemplars who combined their political and literary careers.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=227|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=143}} To manage the stress, he took up jogging and cycling,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=228}} and became so well known for the latter that Gimson suggested he was \"perhaps the most famous cyclist in Britain\".{{sfn|Gimson|2012|p=182}}\n\nFollowing [[William Hague]]'s resignation as Conservative leader, Johnson backed [[Kenneth Clarke]], regarding Clarke as the only candidate capable of winning a general election; the party elected [[Iain Duncan Smith]].{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=223\u2013224|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=150}} Johnson had a strained relationship with Duncan Smith, and ''The Spectator'' became critical of his party leadership.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=234|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=150}} Duncan Smith was removed from his position in November 2003 and replaced by [[Michael Howard]]; Howard deemed Johnson to be the most popular Conservative politician with the electorate and appointed him vice-chairman of the party, responsible for overseeing its electoral campaign.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1pp=46\u201347|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2pp=242\u2013243|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3p=178}} In his Shadow Cabinet reshuffle of May 2004, Howard appointed Johnson to the position of shadow arts minister.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=243|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=179}} In October, Howard ordered Johnson to apologise publicly in [[Liverpool]] for publishing a ''Spectator'' article{{emdash}}anonymously written by [[Simon Heffer]] \u2013 which said the crowds at the [[Hillsborough disaster]] had contributed towards the incident and that Liverpudlians had a predilection for reliance on the [[Welfare state in the United Kingdom|welfare state]].{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=47|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2pp=251\u2013252|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3pp=193\u2013207}}<ref>{{Cite news |date=19 October 2004 |title=Boris sorry for Scouse stereotype |work=BBC News |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/uk_news/politics/3756418.stm |access-date=9 April 2020 |archive-date=9 April 2020 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20200409193955/http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/uk_news/politics/3756418.stm |url-status=live }}</ref>\n\nIn November 2004, tabloids revealed that since 2000 Johnson had been having an affair with ''Spectator'' columnist [[Petronella Wyatt]], resulting in two terminated pregnancies. Johnson initially called the claims \"an inverted pyramid of piffle\".{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=47|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2pp=257\u2013263|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3pp=162\u2013173, 209\u2013218}} After the allegations were proven, Howard asked Johnson to resign as vice-chairman and shadow arts minister for publicly lying; when Johnson refused, Howard dismissed him from those positions.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=47|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2pp=265\u2013267|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3pp=222\u2013223}}<ref>{{Cite news |last=McSmith |first=Andy |date=14 November 2004 |title=On your bike, Boris: Howard sacks Johnson over private life |work=[[The Independent]] |location=London |url=http://news.independent.co.uk/uk/politics/story.jsp?story=582750 |access-date=12 September 2013 |url-status=dead |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20041213175715/http://news.independent.co.uk/uk/politics/story.jsp?story=582750 |archive-date=13 December 2004}}</ref> In July 2005, ''Who's the Daddy?'', a play by ''The Spectator''{{'}}s theatre critics [[Toby Young]] and Lloyd Evans being performed at Islington's [[King's Head Theatre]], satirised the scandal.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=277\u2013281|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=232\u2013233}}\n\n====Second term====\n[[File:University Park MMB 21 Portland Building - Boris Johnson.jpg|thumb|right|As Shadow Minister for Higher Education, Johnson visited various universities (as here at the [[University of Nottingham]] in 2006)]]\n[[File:Boris Johnson cropped.JPG|thumb|Johnson in 2007]]\nIn the [[2005 United Kingdom general election|2005 general election]], Johnson was re-elected MP for Henley, increasing his majority to 12,793.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=277|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=232}} Labour won the election and Howard stood down as Conservative leader; Johnson backed [[David Cameron]] as his successor.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=283\u2013284|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=235\u2013236}} After Cameron was elected, he appointed Johnson as the shadow higher education minister, acknowledging his popularity among students.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=47|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2p=289|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3p=243}} Interested in streamlining university funding,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=302}} Johnson supported Labour's [[Tuition fees in the United Kingdom|proposed top-up fees]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=291}} He campaigned in 2006 to become the [[Rector of the University of Edinburgh]], but his support for top-up fees damaged his campaign, and he came third.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=291\u2013292|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=248}}<ref name=\"HES24Feb06\">{{Cite news |last=Fazackerley |first=Anna |date=24 February 2006 |title=Blond has more fun but fails to thwart anti top-up fee vote |work=[[Times Higher Education Supplement]] |location=London |url=http://www.timeshighereducation.co.uk/story.asp?storyCode=201555&sectioncode=26 |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20120514070050/http://www.timeshighereducation.co.uk/story.asp?storyCode=201555&sectioncode=26 |archive-date=14 May 2012}}</ref>\n\nIn April 2006, the ''[[News of the World]]'' alleged that Johnson was having an affair with the journalist Anna Fazackerley; the pair did not comment, and shortly afterwards Johnson began employing Fazackerley.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=296\u2013300|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=255\u2013257}}<ref name=\"auto\">{{Cite news |last=Barber |first=Lynn |date=19 October 2008 |title=No more Mr Nice Guy |work=The Guardian |location=London |url=https://www.theguardian.com/culture/2008/oct/19/boris-london |access-date=30 July 2022 |archive-date=6 February 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210206061751/https://www.theguardian.com/culture/2008/oct/19/boris-london |url-status=live }}</ref> That month, he attracted further public attention for rugby-tackling former footballer [[Maurizio Gaudino]] in a charity football match.{{sfn|Gimson|2012|p=260}} In September 2006, [[Papua New Guinea]]'s [[High Commission]] protested after he compared the Conservatives' frequently changing leadership to [[Cannibalism#New Guinea|cannibalism in Papua New Guinea]].{{sfn|Gimson|2012|p=266}}\n\nIn 2005, ''The Spectator''{{'}}s new chief executive, [[Andrew Neil]], dismissed Johnson as editor.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=289\u2013290|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=244\u2013245}} To make up for this loss of income, Johnson negotiated with ''The Daily Telegraph'' to raise his annual fee from \u00a3200,000 to \u00a3250,000, averaging at \u00a35,000 per column, each of which took up around an hour and a half of his time.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=294\u2013295}}<ref>{{Cite news |last=Brook, Stephen |date=15 May 2008 |title=Boris to return to Telegraph column |work=The Guardian |location=London |url=https://www.theguardian.com/media/2008/may/15/dailytelegraph.pressandpublishing |access-date=7 July 2010 |archive-date=6 February 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210206061804/https://www.theguardian.com/media/2008/may/15/dailytelegraph.pressandpublishing |url-status=live }}</ref> He presented a [[popular history]] television show, ''[[The Dream of Rome]]'', which was broadcast in January 2006; a book followed in February.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=292\u2013293|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=250\u2013253}} A sequel, ''After Rome'', focused on early Islamic history.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=294}} As a result of his various activities, in 2007, he earned \u00a3540,000, making him the UK's third-highest-earning MP that year.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=295}}\n\n==Mayor of London (2008\u20132016)==\n{{Main|Mayoralty of Boris Johnson}}\n\n===Mayoral election: 2007\u20132008===\n{{main|2008 London mayoral election}}\n{{See also|2007 London Conservative Party mayoral selection}}\n\nIn July 2007, Johnson announced his candidacy to be the Conservative candidate for [[Mayor of London]] in the 2008 mayoral election.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=52|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2p=312|3a1=Gimson|3y=2012|3pp=278\u2013279}}<ref>{{Cite news |last=Jones |first=George |date=16 July 2007 |title=Boris Johnson to run for mayor |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/uknews/1557547/Boris-Johnson-to-run-for-mayor.html |url-status=live |work=The Daily Telegraph |location=London |archive-url=https://archive.today/2013.05.05-084351/http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/uknews/1557547/Boris-Johnson-to-run-for-mayor.html |archive-date=5 May 2013 |access-date=5 May 2021 |url-access=limited}}</ref> In September, he was selected after gaining 79 per cent of the vote in a public London-wide [[Primary election|primary]].{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=53|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=279}}<ref>{{Cite news |date=27 September 2007 |title=Johnson is Tory mayor candidate |work=BBC News |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/uk_politics/7014739.stm |access-date=2 January 2010 |archive-date=13 April 2008 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20080413185715/http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/uk_politics/7014739.stm |url-status=live }}</ref>\n\n[[File:Boris Johnson -holding a red model bus -2007.jpg|thumb|right|Johnson pledged to replace the city's [[Articulated buses in London|articulated buses]] with [[New Routemaster]] buses if elected mayor]]\n\nJohnson's mayoral campaign focused on reducing youth crime, making public transport safer, and replacing the [[Articulated buses in London|articulated buses]] with an updated version of the [[AEC Routemaster]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=327}} Targeting the Conservative-leaning suburbs of [[outer London]], it capitalised on perceptions that the Labour Mayoralty had neglected them in favour of [[inner London]].{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=327|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=285}} His campaign emphasised his popularity, even among those who opposed his policies,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=326\u2013327}} with opponents complaining a common attitude among voters was: \"I'm voting for Boris because he is a laugh.\"{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=327}} The campaign of Labour incumbent [[Ken Livingstone]] portrayed Johnson as an out-of-touch [[toff]] and bigot, citing racist and homophobic language used in his columns; Johnson responded these quotes had been taken out of context and were meant as satire.{{sfnm|1a1=Hosken|1y=2008|1pp=426\u2013427|2a1=Edwards|2a2=Isaby|2y=2008|2pp=67\u201369, 133|3a1=Purnell|3y=2011|3pp=90, 315}}\n\nIn the election, Johnson received 43% and Livingstone 37% of first-preference votes; when second-preference votes were added, Johnson proved victorious with 53% to Livingstone's 47%.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1pp=200\u2013201|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2p=348}}<ref name=\"bbcmayorwin\">{{Cite news |date=3 May 2008 |title=Johnson wins London mayoral race |work=BBC News |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/uk_politics/7380947.stm |access-date=3 May 2008 |archive-date=9 August 2017 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20170809215058/http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/uk_politics/7380947.stm |url-status=live }}</ref> Johnson then announced his resignation as MP for Henley.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=351}}<ref>{{Cite news |last=Watt |first=Nicholas |date=3 May 2008 |title=Johnson snatches Tories' biggest prize |work=The Guardian |location=London |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2008/may/03/london08.boris1 |access-date=1 April 2010 |archive-date=6 February 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210206061843/https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2008/may/03/london08.boris1 |url-status=live }}</ref>\n\n===First term: 2008\u20132012===\nAfter Johnson became mayor, those in City Hall who were deemed too closely allied to Livingstone's administration had their employment terminated.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=366}} Johnson appointed [[Tim Parker]] to be first Deputy Mayor, but after Parker began taking increasing control at City Hall and insisted that all staff report directly to him, Johnson dismissed him.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=368\u2013271}} Because of these problems, many in the Conservative Party initially distanced themselves from Johnson's administration, fearing it would be counterproductive to achieving a Conservative victory in the [[2010 United Kingdom general election|2010 general election]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=368}}\n\n[[File:Boris Johnson on the podium.jpg|thumb|right|Johnson gave a victory speech in [[City Hall, Southwark|City Hall]] after being elected as the [[Mayor of London]] ]]\n\nDuring the electoral campaign, Johnson had confided to [[Brian Paddick]] he was unsure how he would maintain his lifestyle while relying upon the mayoral salary of \u00a3140,000 a year.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=360}} To resolve this problem, he agreed to continue his ''Daily Telegraph'' column alongside his mayoral job, thus earning a further \u00a3250,000 a year.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=361}} His team believed this would cause controversy and made him promise to donate a fifth of his ''Daily Telegraph'' fee to a charitable cause providing bursaries for students. Johnson resented this, and ultimately did not pay a full fifth.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=362}} Controversy erupted when he was questioned about his ''Daily Telegraph'' fee on [[BBC]]'s ''[[HARDtalk]]''; there, he referred to the \u00a3250,000 as \"chicken feed\", something that was widely condemned, given that this was roughly 10 times the average yearly wage for a British worker.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=363}}<ref>{{Cite news |date=14 July 2009 |title=Mayor's \u00a3250,000 'chicken feed' |work=BBC News |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/england/london/8148899.stm |access-date=2 January 2010 |archive-date=13 January 2022 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220113000358/http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/england/london/8148899.stm |url-status=live }}</ref><ref>{{Cite news |last=Mulholland |first=H\u00e9l\u00e8ne |date=14 July 2009 |title=Johnson condemned for describing \u00a3250,000 deal as 'chicken feed' |work=The Guardian |location=London |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2009/jul/14/boris-johnson-telegraph-chicken-feed |access-date=1 April 2010 |archive-date=6 February 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210206061858/https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2009/jul/14/boris-johnson-telegraph-chicken-feed |url-status=live }}</ref>\n[[File:Boris Johnson -opening bell at NASDAQ-14Sept2009-3c.jpg|thumb|Johnson at the opening of NASDAQ in 2009]]\nDuring his first administration, Johnson was embroiled in several personal scandals. After moving to a new house in Islington, he built a shed on his balcony without obtaining [[planning permission]]; after neighbours complained, he dismantled it.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=396\u2013397|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=308}} The press also accused him of having an affair with Helen Macintyre and of fathering her child, allegations that he did not deny.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=407\u2013410|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=330\u2013331}}<ref name=\"theguardian2\">{{Cite news |last=Mulholland |first=Helene |date=15 December 2010 |title=No censure for Boris Johnson over relationship with unpaid City Hall adviser |work=The Guardian |location=London |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2010/dec/15/no-censure-boris-johnson-city-hall-adviser |access-date=30 July 2022 |archive-date=2 December 2020 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20201202040258/https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2010/dec/15/no-censure-boris-johnson-city-hall-adviser |url-status=live }}</ref><ref>{{Cite news |last=Mulholland |first=H\u00e9l\u00e8ne |date=20 July 2010 |title=Boris Johnson pressed for full details of the appointment of his alleged lover |work=The Guardian |location=London |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2010/jul/20/boris-johnson-mayor-2012-macintyre |access-date=30 July 2022 |archive-date=6 February 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210206062215/https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2010/jul/20/boris-johnson-mayor-2012-macintyre |url-status=live }}</ref><ref name=\"Telegraph210513\">{{Cite news |date=21 May 2013 |title=Public has right to know about Boris Johnson's secret lovechild, court rules |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/uknews/10070055/Public-has-right-to-know-about-Boris-Johnsons-secret-lovechild-court-rules.html |url-status=live |work=The Daily Telegraph |location=London |archive-url=https://archive.today/2013.06.16-032220/http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/uknews/10070055/Public-has-right-to-know-about-Boris-Johnsons-secret-lovechild-court-rules.html |archive-date=16 June 2013 |access-date=5 May 2021 |url-access=limited}}</ref> Controversy arose when Johnson was accused of warning the MP [[Damian Green]] that police were planning to arrest him; Johnson denied the claims and did not face criminal charges under the [[Criminal Justice Act]].{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=380\u2013385|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=305\u2013308}} He was accused of [[cronyism]],{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=407}} in particular for appointing [[Veronica Wadley]], a former ''Evening Standard'' editor who had supported him, as the chair of London's [[Arts Council]] when she was widely regarded as not being the best candidate for the position.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=401\u2013402}}<ref>{{Cite news |last=Mulholland |first=H\u00e9l\u00e8ne |date=9 October 2009 |title=Ken Livingstone claims Boris Johnson tried to 'pay off' former Evening Standard editor |work=The Guardian |location=London |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2009/oct/09/boris-johnson-ken-livingstone-standard |access-date=30 July 2022 |archive-date=6 February 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210206062227/https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2009/oct/09/boris-johnson-ken-livingstone-standard |url-status=live }}</ref><ref>{{Cite news |last=Coates |first=Sam |date=8 October 2009 |title=Boris Johnson 'broke rules' by proposing ally for top London arts job |work=[[The Times]] |location=London |url=https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/boris-johnson-broke-rules-by-proposing-ally-for-top-london-arts-job-bj57sz2kgtv |url-access=subscription |access-date=16 April 2020 |archive-date=1 August 2020 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20200801060948/https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/boris-johnson-broke-rules-by-proposing-ally-for-top-london-arts-job-bj57sz2kgtv |url-status=live }}</ref> He was caught up in the [[United Kingdom parliamentary expenses scandal|parliamentary expenses scandal]] and accused of excessive personal spending on taxi journeys. His deputy mayor [[Ian Clement]] was found to have misused a City Hall credit card, resulting in his resignation.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=392\u2013395}}\nJohnson remained a popular figure in London with a strong celebrity status.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=446}} In 2009, he rescued [[Franny Armstrong]] from anti-social teenagers who had threatened her while he was cycling past them.{{sfn|Gimson|2012|p=308}}<ref>{{Cite news |date=3 November 2009 |title=Johnson saves woman from 'oiks' |work=BBC News |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/england/london/8340865.stm |access-date=7 November 2009 |archive-date=1 March 2020 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20200301104808/http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/england/london/8340865.stm |url-status=live }}</ref><ref>{{Cite news |last=Mayer |first=Catherine |author-link=Catherine Mayer |date=4 November 2009 |title=London Mayor Boris Johnson Saves Filmmaker from Mugging |magazine=Time |location=New York |url=http://www.time.com/time/world/article/0,8599,1934572,00.html |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20091107031219/http://www.time.com/time/world/article/0,8599,1934572,00.html |url-status=dead |archive-date=7 November 2009 |access-date=7 November 2009}}</ref>\n\n====Policies====\n[[File:Arriva London bus LT2 (LT61 BHT) 2011 New Bus for London, Victoria bus station, route 38, 27 February 2012 (1).jpg|thumb|right|The [[New Routemaster]] bus introduced by Johnson's administration]]\n\nJohnson made no major changes to the mayoral system Livingstone developed.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=373}} He reversed several measures implemented by Livingstone's administration, ending the city's oil deal with Venezuela, abolishing ''[[The Londoner]]'' newsletter, and scrapping the half-yearly inspections of [[Hackney carriage|black cabs]], which was reinstated three years later.{{sfnm|1a1=Edwards|1a2=Isaby|1y=2008|1p=211|2a1=Purnell|2y=2011|2p=373}} Abolishing the western wing of the [[London congestion charge|congestion charging]] zone,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=443}} he cancelled plans to increase the congestion charge for [[four-wheel drive|four-wheel-drive vehicles]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=391}} He was subsequently accused of failing to publish an independent report on air pollution commissioned by the [[Greater London Authority]], which revealed the city breached legal limits on [[nitrogen dioxide]] levels.<ref name=\"tele\">{{Cite news |date=17 May 2016 |title=Boris Johnson accused of hiding study linking air pollution and deprived schools |work=The Daily Telegraph |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2016/05/17/boris-johndon-accused-of-hiding-study-linking-air-pollution-and/ |archive-url=https://ghostarchive.org/archive/20220110/https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2016/05/17/boris-johndon-accused-of-hiding-study-linking-air-pollution-and/ |archive-date=10 January 2022 |url-status=live |url-access=limited |access-date=5 May 2021}}{{cbignore}}</ref><ref>{{Cite news |last=Mortimer |first=Caroline |date=16 May 2016 |title=Boris Johnson accused of burying report on the number of schools in London's most polluted areas |work=The Independent |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/boris-johnson-accused-of-burying-report-on-the-number-of-deprived-schools-in-londons-most-polluted-a7033016.html}}</ref>\n\nJohnson retained Livingstone projects such as [[Crossrail]] and the [[2012 Summer Olympics|2012 Olympic Games]], but was accused of trying to take credit for them.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=390}} He introduced a public bicycle scheme that had been mooted by Livingstone's administration; colloquially known as \"[[Santander Cycles|Boris Bikes]]\", the part privately financed system cost \u00a3140&nbsp;million and was a significant financial loss but proved popular.<ref>{{cite web |last=Hoscik |first=Martin |date=19 December 2012 |title=Exclusive: TfL reveals how much Barclays has paid for Cycle Hire scheme |url=http://www.mayorwatch.co.uk/exclusive-tfl-reveals-how-much-barclays-has-paid-for-cycle-hire-scheme/201223704/ |website=MayorWatch}}</ref>{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=414\u2013416|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=307}} Despite Johnson's support of cycling in London, and his much-publicised identity as a cyclist, some cycling groups who argued he had failed to make the city's roads safer for cyclists criticised his administration.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=442\u2013443}} As per his election pledge, he commissioned the development of the [[New Routemaster]] buses for central London.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=417|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=307}} He also ordered the construction of a [[Emirates Air Line (cable car)|cable car system]] that crossed the [[River Thames]] between [[Greenwich Peninsula]] and the [[Royal Docks]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=417\u2013418}}\n\n[[File:BorisBikes.jpg|left|thumb|Johnson implemented Livingstone's idea of a public bicycle system; the result was dubbed the [[Santander Cycles|\"Boris Bike\"]].]]\n\nJohnson's first policy initiative was a ban on drinking alcohol on public transport.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=265|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=288}}<ref name=\"lgovpr20080507\">{{cite press release |title=Mayor unveils plan to ban alcohol on the transport network |date=6 May 2008 |publisher=Greater London Authority |url=http://www.london.gov.uk/view_press_release.jsp?releaseid=16793 |access-date=7 May 2008 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20080513075018/http://www.london.gov.uk/view_press_release.jsp?releaseid=16793 |archive-date=13 May 2008}}</ref> At the beginning of his tenure as mayor, Johnson announced plans to extend pay-as-you-go [[Oyster cards]] to national rail services in London.<ref name=\"es_oystersie\">{{cite news |last=Waugh |first=Paul |date=12 May 2008 |title=Boris plans to 'Oysterise' overground rail services by next May |work=Evening Standard |url=https://www.standard.co.uk/news/boris-plans-to-oysterise-overground-rail-services-by-next-may-6690497.html |access-date=21 November 2013}}</ref> A pledge in Johnson's election manifesto was to retain Tube ticket offices, in opposition to Livingstone's proposal to close up to 40 London Underground ticket offices.<ref name=\"2008_transport_manifesto\">{{cite news |last=Johnson |first=Boris |title=Saying No To Ticket Office Closures |page=38 |work=Getting Londoners Moving (Transport Manifesto for the 2008&nbsp;Mayoral election) |publisher=Back Boris campaign |url=http://image.guardian.co.uk/sys-files/Guardian/documents/2009/04/27/Transportmanifesto.pdf |access-date=21 November 2013}}</ref> On
