2&nbsp;July 2008, the Mayor's office announced the closure plan was to be abandoned and that offices would remain open.<ref name=\"es_kenplan\">{{cite news |last=Murray |first=Dick |date=2 July 2008 |title=Mayor scraps Ken plan to axe 40 Tube ticket offices |work=Evening Standard |url=https://www.standard.co.uk/news/mayor-scraps-ken-plan-to-axe-40-tube-ticket-offices-6869816.html |access-date=21 November 2013}}</ref> On 21&nbsp;November 2013, [[Transport for London]] announced that all London Underground ticket offices would close by 2015.<ref name=\"bbc_borisplan\">{{cite news |date=21 November 2013 |title=London Underground in 24-hour plans as ticket offices shut |work=BBC News |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-england-london-25025888 |access-date=17 February 2015}}</ref> In financing these projects, Johnson's administration borrowed \u00a3100&nbsp;million,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=437}} while public transport fares were increased by 50 per cent.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=416}}\n\nDuring his first Mayoral term, Johnson was perceived as having moved leftward on certain issues, for instance, supporting the [[Living wage in the United Kingdom|London Living Wage]] and endorsing an amnesty for illegal migrants.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=388}} He tried placating critics who had deemed him a bigot by appearing at [[Pride London|London's gay pride parade]] and praising ethnic minority newspapers.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=374\u2013375}} In 2012, he banned London buses from displaying the adverts of [[Core Issues Trust]], a Christian group, which compared homosexuality to an illness.<ref>{{cite news |last1=Mulholland |first1=Helene |last2=Booth |first2=Robert |last3=Strudwick |first3=Patrick |date=12 April 2012 |title=Anti-gay adverts pulled from bus campaign by Boris Johnson |work=The Guardian |location=London |url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2012/apr/12/anti-gay-adverts-boris-johnson}}</ref> In August 2008, Johnson broke from the traditional protocol of those in public office not publicly commenting on other nations' elections by endorsing [[Barack Obama]] for the [[2008 United States presidential election|presidency of the United States]].<ref>{{cite news |last=Mulholland |first=H\u00e9l\u00e8ne |date=1 August 2008 |title=Barack Obama gets backing from Boris Johnson |work=The Guardian |location=London |url=https://www.theguardian.com/global/2008/aug/01/boris.barackobama |access-date=1 April 2010}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |last=Prince |first=Rosa |date=1 August 2008 |title=Boris Johnson backs Barack Obama as US President |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/politics/london-mayor-election/mayor-of-london/2484162/Boris-Johnson-backs-Barack-Obama-as-US-President.html |url-status=live |work=The Daily Telegraph |location=London |archive-url=https://archive.today/2012.09.12-005110/http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/politics/london-mayor-election/mayor-of-london/2484162/Boris-Johnson-backs-Barack-Obama-as-US-President.html |archive-date=12 September 2012 |access-date=5 May 2021 |url-access=limited}}</ref>\n\n====Relations with police, finance, and the media====\n[[File:2011 London riots.jpg|thumb|right|Johnson's response to the 2011 London riots was criticised]]\nJohnson appointed himself chair of the [[Metropolitan Police Authority]] (MPA), and in October 2008 successfully pushed for the resignation of Metropolitan Police Commissioner [[Ian Blair]] after the latter was criticised for allegedly handing contracts to friends and for his handling of the [[death of Jean Charles de Menezes]].{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=377\u2013278|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=304}}<ref>{{cite news |date=2 October 2008 |title=Britain's top policeman resigns |work=BBC News |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/uk/7648664.stm |access-date=1 April 2010}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |last1=O'Neill |first1=Sean |last2=Fresco |first2=Adam |last3=Coates |first3=Sam |date=3 October 2008 |title=Boris Johnson forces Sir Ian Blair to quit as police chief |work=The Times |location=London |url=https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/boris-johnson-forces-sir-ian-blair-to-quit-as-police-chief-qcmtwr7ff70 |url-access=subscription |access-date=16 April 2020}}</ref> This earned Johnson great respect among Conservatives, who interpreted it as his first act of strength.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=379}} Johnson resigned as MPA chairman in January 2010,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=388}} but throughout his mayoralty was highly supportive of the Metropolitan Police, particularly during the controversy surrounding the [[death of Ian Tomlinson]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=387\u2013388}} Overall crime in London fell during his administration, but his claim that serious youth crime had decreased proved to be false, and he acknowledged the error.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=436}}<ref>{{Cite news |last=Donovan |first=Tim |date=16 November 2011 |title=London mayor admits 'caveats' in youth crime statistics |work=BBC News |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-england-london-15765557}}</ref> Similarly, his claim that Metropolitan Police numbers had increased was characterised as untrue,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=436}} but the fact-checkers at [[Full Fact]] felt that both Johnson's and his critics' positions are defensible.<ref>{{cite web |url=https://fullfact.org/crime/police-officers-under-boris-johnson/ |title=Police numbers under Boris Johnson's time as Mayor |work=[[Full Fact]] |date=26 July 2019 |access-date=24 September 2020}}</ref> He was criticised for his response to the [[2011 England riots|2011 London riots]]; holidaying with his family in [[British Columbia]] when the rioting broke out, he did not return immediately to London, only doing so 48 hours after it had begun and addressing Londoners 60 hours thereafter. Upon visiting shopkeepers and residents affected by the riots in [[Clapham]], elements within the crowds booed and jeered him.{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1pp=429\u2013432|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2pp=333\u2013335}}\n\n[[File:BorisOC2010.jpg|thumb|left|Johnson lights the flame at the 2010 [[London Youth Games]] opening ceremony]]\n\nJohnson championed London's financial sector and denounced what he saw as \"banker bashing\" following the [[financial crisis of 2007\u201308]],{{sfnm|1a1=Purnell|1y=2011|1p=400|2a1=Gimson|2y=2012|2p=324}} condemning the [[anti-capitalism|anti-capitalist]] [[Occupy London]] movement that appeared in 2011.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=439\u2013440}} He spent much time with those involved in the financial services and criticised the government's 50p tax rate for higher earners.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=400}} He collected donations from the city's wealthy for a charitable enterprise, the Mayor's Fund, which he had established to aid disadvantaged youths. It initially announced the fund would raise \u00a3100&nbsp;million, but by 2010 it had only spent \u00a31.5&nbsp;million.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=414}} He also maintained extensive personal contacts throughout the British media,{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=389}} which resulted in widespread favourable press coverage of his administration.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=389}} In turn he remained largely supportive of his friends in the media{{emdash}}among them [[Rupert Murdoch|Rupert Murdoch{{emdash}}]]<nowiki/>during the [[News International phone hacking scandal]].{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=424\u2013425}}\n\nThe formation of the Forensic Audit Panel was announced on 8&nbsp;May 2008. The panel was tasked with monitoring and investigating financial management at the [[London Development Agency]] and the [[Greater London Authority]].<ref name=\"lgovpr20080508\">{{Cite press release |title=Mayor of London announces new Forensic Audit Panel to investigate GLA and LDA |date=8 May 2008 |publisher=Greater London Authority |url=http://www.london.gov.uk/view_press_release.jsp?releaseid=16814 |access-date=7 May 2008 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20080518065203/http://www.london.gov.uk/view_press_release.jsp?releaseid=16814 |archive-date=18 May 2008}}</ref> Johnson's announcement was criticised by Labour for the perceived politicisation of this nominally independent panel, who asked whether the appointment of key Johnson allies to the panel \u2013 \"to dig dirt on Ken Livingstone\" \u2013 was \"an appropriate use of public funds\".<ref>{{Cite web |date=9 May 2008 |title=Labour accuse Mayor of 'Tory witch hunt' |url=http://www.mayorwatch.co.uk/Labour-Accuse-Mayor-of-Tory-Witch-Hunt-article_id-1616.html |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20080513014713/http://www.mayorwatch.co.uk/Labour-Accuse-Mayor-of-Tory-Witch-Hunt-article_id-1616.html |archive-date=13 May 2008 |access-date=12 September 2013 |publisher=MayorWatch}}</ref> The head of the panel, [[Patience Wheatcroft]], was married to a Conservative councillor<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.theguardian.com/media/2006/jul/17/mediaguardiantop100200638 |title=The Media Guardian 100 \u2013 87 Patience Wheatcroft |date=17 July 2006 |newspaper=[[The Guardian]] |location=London |issn=0261-3077}}</ref> and three of the four remaining panel members also had close links to the Conservatives: [[Stephen Greenhalgh]] (Conservative Leader of [[Hammersmith and Fulham London Borough Council]]),<ref>{{Cite web |title=Stephen Greenhalgh \u2013 Conservative Home |url=http://www.conservativehome.com/tag/stephen-greenhalgh |access-date=25 June 2016}}</ref> Patrick Frederick (Chairman of Conservative Business Relations for South East England and Southern London), and Edward Lister (Conservative Leader of [[Wandsworth London Borough Council]]).<ref>{{Cite news |last=Barney |first=Katharine |date=15 July 2008 |title=Ken Livingstone refuses to appear before LDA audit panel |work=Evening Standard |url=https://www.standard.co.uk/news/mayor/ken-livingstone-refuses-to-appear-before-lda-audit-panel-6863880.html |access-date=25 June 2016}}</ref>\n\n====Re-election campaign====\nUp for re-election in 2012, Johnson again hired Crosby to orchestrate his campaign.{{sfn|Gimson|2012|p=338}}\nBefore the election, Johnson published ''Johnson's Life of London'', a work of popular history that the historian [[A. N. Wilson]] characterised as a \"coded plea\" for votes.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|pp=447\u2013448}}\nPolls suggested that while Livingstone's approach to transport was preferred, voters in London placed greater trust in Johnson over issues of crime and the economy.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=440}} During the [[2012 London mayoral election|2012 Mayoral election]], Johnson sought re-election, while Livingstone was selected as the Labour candidate again. Johnson's campaign emphasised the accusation that Livingstone was guilty of [[tax evasion]], for which Livingstone called Johnson a \"bare-faced liar\".{{sfn|Crines|2013|p=3}} The political scientist Andrew Crines believed that Livingstone's campaign focused on criticising Johnson rather than presenting an alternate and progressive vision of London's future.{{sfn|Crines|2013|p=2}} In 2012, Johnson was [[2012 London mayoral election|re-elected]] as mayor.<ref name=\"BBC5May12\">{{Cite news |date=5 May 2012 |title=London mayor: Boris Johnson wins second term by tight margin 5&nbsp;May 2012 Last updated at 01:35 |work=BBC News |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-17946742 |access-date=5 May 2012}}</ref>\n\n===Second term: 2012\u20132016===\n[[File:Boris 2012.jpg|thumb|right|Johnson at the [[2012 Summer Olympics]]]]\nLondon was successful in its bid to host the [[2012 Summer Olympics]] while Ken Livingstone was still mayor in 2005. Johnson's role in the proceedings was as the co-chair of an Olympic board which oversaw the games.<ref>{{Cite web |title=The Mayor of London \u2013 2012 Olympics |url=http://www.london2012.com/about-us/the-people-delivering-the-games/stakeholders/the-mayor-of-london/ |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20120729172513/http://www.london2012.com/about-us/the-people-delivering-the-games/stakeholders/the-mayor-of-london/ |archive-date=29 July 2012 |access-date=11 December 2012 |publisher=London 2012}}</ref> Two of his actions after taking on this role were to improve transportation around London by making more tickets available and laying on more buses around the capital during the busy period, when thousands of spectators were temporary visitors in London.<ref>{{Cite news |date=30 July 2012 |title=Boris Johnson on London 2012 Olympics tickets and transport |work=The Guardian (video) |url=https://www.theguardian.com/sport/video/2012/jul/30/boris-johnson-olympics-tickets-transport-video |access-date=11 December 2012}}</ref><ref>{{Cite news |date=22 July 2012 |title=London 2012: Boris Johnson says capital is prepared |work=BBC News |url=http://bbc.co.uk/news/uk-18944191 |access-date=11 December 2012}}</ref> Johnson was accused of covering up pollution ahead of the games by deploying dust suppressants to remove air particulates near monitoring stations.<ref name=tele/> In November 2013, Johnson announced major changes to the operation of [[London Underground]], including the extension of operating hours to run through the night at weekends. All staffed ticket offices would be closed to save over \u00a340&nbsp;million a year and replaced with automated ticketing systems.<ref name=\"bbc_tube_ticket\">{{Cite news |date=21 November 2013 |title=Every Tube ticket office to close |work=BBC News |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-england-london-25025888 |access-date=21 November 2013}}</ref><ref name=\"es_shakeup\">{{Cite news |last=Beard |first=Matthew |date=21 November 2013 |title=950 London Underground staff to lose their jobs in Tube ticket office shake-up |work=Evening Standard |url=https://www.standard.co.uk/news/transport/950-london-underground-staff-to-lose-their-jobs-in-tube-ticket-office-shakeup-8954060.html |access-date=21 November 2013}}</ref>\n\nJohnson had a close friendship with American technology entrepreneur, former DJ<ref>{{Cite news |date=23 September 2019 |title=PM Defends Actions over Conflict of Interest Claims |work=BBC News |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49794693}}</ref> and model [[Jennifer Arcuri]], with ''[[The Sunday Times]]'' describing him as a regular visitor to her flat,<ref>{{Cite news |last=Bulman |first=May |date=22 September 2019 |title=Boris Johnson facing questions over relationship with ex-model Jennifer Arcuri |work=The Independent |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/home-news/boris-johnson-jennifer-arcuri-model-london-mayor-friendship-conflict-interest-a9115211.html |access-date=26 September 2019}}</ref> and implying they were in a sexual relationship.<ref name=\"sky\">{{Cite news |last=Coates |first=Sam |date=25 September 2019 |title=Boris Johnson breaks silence on relationship with entrepreneur Jennifer Arcuri |publisher=[[Sky News]] |url=https://news.sky.com/story/boris-johnson-refuses-six-times-to-answer-questions-about-funding-for-close-friend-11817579}}</ref> In 2013, a mayoral fund awarded her company, Innotech, \u00a310,000, followed the next year by Arcuri being awarded \u00a315,000 from a government programme. Johnson intervened to allow her onto three trade mission trips.<ref>{{Cite web |date=22 September 2019 |title=Boris Johnson Facing Questions over Giving Public Money to American Woman |url=https://www.huffingtonpost.co.uk/entry/boris-johnson-jennifer-arcuri_uk_5d8794f3e4b0849d472ad0dc |website=HuffPost}}</ref> ''The Sunday Times'' said in September 2019 that Johnson failed to declare his personal relationship as a conflict of interest.<ref>{{Cite news |last=Weaver |first=Matthew |date=22 September 2019 |title=Boris Johnson urged to justify 'awarding public funds to close friend' |work=The Guardian |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/sep/22/boris-johnson-urged-to-justify-awarding-public-funds-to-close-friend-jennifer-arcuri |access-date=26 September 2019}}</ref> Later that month, the Greater London Authority referred Johnson and his actions in the matter to the [[Independent Office for Police Conduct]] (IOPC) \"so it can assess whether or not it is necessary to investigate the former mayor of London for the criminal offence of misconduct in public office\". The IOPC was involved because the Mayor is also London's [[police and crime commissioner]].<ref>{{Cite news |date=27 September 2019 |title=PM's links to Arcuri referred to police watchdog |work=BBC News |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-49859321 }}</ref> The [[London Assembly]] began its own investigation but paused it at the IOPC's request to avoid overlap. On 9&nbsp;November 2019 it was revealed that the IOPC, which had been due to publish a report on its investigation, had decided to do so after the general election of 12&nbsp;December.<ref>{{Cite news |last=Townsend |first=Mark |date=10 November 2019 |title=Fury as decision on police inquiry into PM shelved until after election |work=The Guardian |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/nov/09/boris-johnson-jennifer-arcuri-iopc-delay-announcement-investigation |access-date=11 November 2019}}</ref> The IOPC issued its report in May 2020, concluding that, although there was no basis for any criminal charge, there was evidence that the close relationship between Johnson and Arcuri had influenced decisions by officials. The report found Johnson should have declared an interest concerning Arcuri and that his failure to do this could have breached the London Assembly's code of conduct. On behalf of the London Assembly, the chair of its Greater London Authority Oversight Committee said the committee would now resume its own investigation.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2020/may/21/boris-johnson-will-not-face-criminal-inquiry-over-jennifer-arcuri|last=Weaver|first=Matthew|title=Boris Johnson will not face criminal inquiry over Jennifer Arcuri|work=The Guardian|date=21 May 2020|access-date=22 May 2020}}<!--clarification of the story:  the \"GLA Oversight Committee\" is a committee of the London Assembly--></ref>\n\nIn 2015, Johnson criticised then-presidential candidate [[Donald Trump]]'s false comments that there were [[No-go area#United Kingdom|no-go zones in London]] governed by [[shariah]] and inaccessible for non-Muslims. Johnson said Trump was \"betraying a quite stupefying ignorance that makes him, frankly, unfit to hold the office of president of the United States\",<ref name=\"Waugh\">{{cite news |last=Waugh |first=Paul |title=Boris slams Trump: he's 'out of his mind' |url=https://www.huffingtonpost.co.uk/2015/12/09/boris-johnson-slams-donal_n_8758292.html |access-date=25 August 2019 |work=HuffPost |date=9 December 2015}}</ref> becoming the first senior politician in the UK to declare Trump unfit for office, but rejecting calls for him to be banned from the country.<ref>{{cite news |last=Dathan |first=Matt |title=Boris Johnson says Donald Trump 'betrays a stupefying ignorance that makes him unfit to be US President' |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/boris-johnson-says-donald-trump-betrays-a-stupefying-ignorance-that-makes-him-unfit-to-be-us-a6766871.html |access-date=25 August 2019 |work=The Independent |date=9 December 2015}}</ref> Johnson added that he \"would invite [Trump] to come and see the whole of London and take him round the city \u2013 except I wouldn't want to expose Londoners to any unnecessary risk of meeting Donald Trump\".<ref name=\"Waugh\" /> He later called Trump's comments \"ill informed\" and \"complete and utter nonsense\", adding that \"the only reason I wouldn't go to some parts of New York is the real risk of meeting Donald Trump\".<ref>{{cite news |last=Horton |first=Helena |date=8 December 2015 |title=Boris Johnson: 'The only reason I wouldn't visit some parts of New York is the real risk of meeting Donald Trump' |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/politics/boris-johnson/12039931/Boris-Johnson-The-only-reason-I-wouldnt-visit-some-parts-of-New-York-is-the-real-risk-of-meeting-Donald-Trump.html |url-status=live |work=The Telegraph |archive-url=https://archive.today/2015.12.14-082438/http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/politics/boris-johnson/12039931/Boris-Johnson-The-only-reason-I-wouldnt-visit-some-parts-of-New-York-is-the-real-risk-of-meeting-Donald-Trump.html |archive-date=14 December 2015 |access-date=5 May 2021 |url-access=limited}}</ref> In 2016, he said he was \"genuinely worried that he could become president\", telling ITV's [[Tom Bradby]] of one moment where he was mistaken for Trump in New York as \"one of the worst moments\" of his life.<ref name=\"Telegraph160321\">{{cite news |last=McCann |first=Kate |date=21 March 2016 |title=Boris Johnson: 'I was mistaken for Donald Trump' |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/politics/boris-johnson/12200562/Boris-Johnson-I-was-mistaken-for-Donald-Trump.html |url-status=live |work=The Telegraph |archive-url=https://archive.today/2016.07.02-131836/http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/politics/boris-johnson/12200562/Boris-Johnson-I-was-mistaken-for-Donald-Trump.html |archive-date=2 July 2016 |access-date=5 May 2021 |url-access=limited}}</ref>\n\nJohnson did not run for a third term for Mayor of London and stepped down on 5&nbsp;May 2016 following [[2016 London mayoral election|the election]] of former transport minister, [[Sadiq Khan]]. Johnson left office still popular with the people of London. A [[YouGov]] poll commissioned at the end of his term revealed that 52% of Londoners believed he did a \"good job\" as Mayor of London while only 29% believed he did a \"bad job\".<ref>{{cite web|url=https://yougov.co.uk/news/2016/05/05/mayor-boris-public-verdict/|title=Mayor Boris: the public verdict |first=Will |last=Dahlgreen |website=[[YouGov]]|access-date=17 July 2018}}</ref> In 2016, Sadiq Khan announced that three German-made water cannon, which Johnson had bought for the [[Metropolitan Police]] without waiting for clearance from the then-Home Secretary [[Theresa May]], were to be sold, with the proceeds going to youth services.<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2016/jul/01/water-cannon-boris-johnson-sold-off-without-being-used |first=Damien |last=Gayle |title=Water cannon bought by Boris Johnson to be sold off without being used |work=The Guardian |date=1 July 2016 |access-date=1 July 2016}}</ref> The vehicles proved unsellable and were eventually sold for scrap in 2018 at a \u00a3300,000 loss.<ref>{{cite news |last=Weaver |first=Matthew |title=Boris Johnson's unused water cannon sold for scrap at \u00a3300,000 loss |url=https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2018/nov/19/boris-johnson-unused-water-cannon-sold-for-scrap-at-300000-loss |work=The Guardian |date=19 November 2018 |access-date=25 November 2018}}</ref>\n\n==Return to Parliament==\nJohnson initially said that he would not return to the House of Commons while remaining mayor.{{sfn|Purnell|2011|p=446}} After much media speculation, in August 2014 he sought selection as the Conservative candidate for the [[safe seat]] of [[Uxbridge and South Ruislip (UK Parliament constituency)|Uxbridge and South Ruislip]] at the [[2015 United Kingdom general election|2015 general election]],<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.standard.co.uk/news/politics/boris-johnson-declares-he-will-stand-in-uxbridge-and-south-ruislip-9691619.html|title=Boris Johnson declares he will stand in Uxbridge and South Ruislip|last=de Peyer|first=Robin|date=26 August 2014|work=Evening Standard|access-date=26 August 2014}}</ref> becoming the party's candidate in September.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2014/sep/12/boris-johnson-tory-candidate-uxbridge-south-ruislip|title=Boris Johnson selected to stand for Tories in Uxbridge and South Ruislip|last=Johnston|first=Chris|date=12 September 2014|work=The Guardian|access-date=30 March 2015|location=London}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|last1=Swinford|first1=Steven|last2=Holehouse|first2=Matthew|date=12 September 2014|title=Boris Johnson selected to stand for Tories in Uxbridge and South Ruislip|url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/general-election-2015/11093857/Boris-Johnson-selected-to-stand-for-Tories-in-Uxbridge-and-South-Ruislip.html|url-status=live|work=The Daily Telegraph|location=London|archive-url=https://archive.today/2015.04.04-211708/http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/general-election-2015/11093857/Boris-Johnson-selected-to-stand-for-Tories-in-Uxbridge-and-South-Ruislip.html|archive-date=4 April 2015|access-date=5 May 2021|url-access=limited}}</ref> In the [[2015 United Kingdom general election|2015 general election]], Johnson was elected MP. There was much speculation that he had returned to Parliament because he wanted to replace Cameron as Conservative leader and prime minister.<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.londonlovesbusiness.com/business-news/politics/boris-johnson-lacks-the-skills-to-be-prime-minister-says-tory-chairman/3431.article|title=Boris Johnson lacks the skills to be prime minister, says Tory chairman|last=Bennett|first=Asa|date=17 September 2012|access-date=8 October 2012|publisher=LondonlovesBusiness.com|archive-date=7 October 2012|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20121007233049/http://www.londonlovesbusiness.com/business-news/politics/boris-johnson-lacks-the-skills-to-be-prime-minister-says-tory-chairman/3431.article|url-status=dead}}</ref>\n\n===Brexit campaign: 2015\u20132016===\n{{Main|Vote Leave}}\n[[File:Boris johnson (cropped).jpg|thumb|Official portrait of Johnson as MP, 2016|245x245px]]\nIn February 2016, Johnson endorsed [[Vote Leave]] in the \"Out\" campaign for the [[2016 United Kingdom European Union membership referendum|2016 European Union membership referendum]].<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-eu-referendum-35626621 |title=Boris Johnson says UK is better off outside the EU |date=21 February 2016 |access-date=21 February 2016 |work=BBC News}}</ref> He called Cameron's warnings about leaving \"greatly over exaggerated\". Following this announcement, which was interpreted by financial markets as making Brexit more probable, the [[pound sterling]] slumped by nearly 2% against the US dollar, reaching its lowest level since March 2009.<ref>{{cite news |last=Wearden |first=Graeme |title=Pound hits seven-year low after Boris Johnson's Brexit decision |url=https://www.theguardian.com/business/live/2016/feb/22/pound-falls-city-gets-brexit-jitters-business-live |access-date=22 February 2016 |work=The Guardian |location=London |date=22 February 2016}}</ref>\n\nIn April 2016, in an article for ''[[The Sun (United Kingdom)|The Sun]]'', in response to a comment by President [[Barack Obama]] that Britain should remain in the European Union, Johnson said an \"ancestral dislike\" of Britain owing to his \"part-Kenyan\" background may have shaped Obama's views.<ref name=\"controversial\">{{cite news |url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/newsbeat/article/36793900/boris-johnsons-most-controversial-foreign-insults |title=Boris Johnson's most controversial foreign insults |work=[[BBC Newsbeat]] |date=14 July 2016 |access-date=18 April 2018}}</ref> Conservative MP Sir [[Nicholas Soames]] branded the comments \"idiotic\" and \"deeply offensive\".<ref name=\"Obama\">{{cite news |url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-36112694 |title=Obama hits back at Boris Johnson's alleged smears |work=BBC News |date=22 April 2016 |access-date=18 April 2018}}</ref> Several Labour and Liberal Democrat politicians condemned them as racist and unacceptable.<ref name=\"ObamaSmear\">{{cite news |title=Obama hits back at Boris Johnson's alleged smears |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-36112694 |access-date=23 April 2016 |work=BBC News |date=22 April 2016}}</ref><ref name=\"ToIApril16\">{{cite news |last=Lawless |first=Jill |title=London mayor under fire for 'loaded' criticism of 'part-Kenyan' Obama |url=http://www.timesofisrael.com/london-mayor-under-fire-for-loaded-criticism-of-part-kenyan-obama/ |access-date=23 April 2016 |work=[[The Times of Israel]] |location=Jerusalem |date=22 April 2016}}</ref> In light of the remark, a [[King's College London]] student society revoked a speaking invitation it had extended to him.<ref>{{cite news |last=Espinoza |first=Javier|date=28 April 2016 |title=Boris Johnson 'no platformed' over Obama's ancestry comments |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/education/2016/04/25/boris-johnson-no-platformed-over-obamas-ancestry-comments/ |url-status=live |work=The Daily Telegraph |location= London |archive-url=https://archive.today/2016.07.18-042526/http://www.telegraph.co.uk/education/2016/04/25/boris-johnson-no-platformed-over-obamas-ancestry-comments/ |archive-date=18 July 2016 |access-date=5 May 2021 |url-access=limited}}</ref> Conversely, the Conservative [[Iain Duncan Smith]] politician and [[UK Independence Party]] (UKIP) leader [[Nigel Farage]] defended his comments.<ref name=\"ObamaSmear\" /><ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/boris-johnson-was-not-racist-to-suggest-barack-obamas-part-kenyan-heritage-made-him-dislike-britain-a6999261.html |title=Boris Johnson was not racist about Barack Obama, Iain Duncan Smith insists |date=25 April 2016 |work=The Independent |location=London |last=Stone |first=Jon |access-date=26 April 2016}}</ref>\n\nJohnson supported Vote Leave's statement that the government was committed to [[Accession of Turkey to the European Union|Turkish accession to the EU]] at the earliest possible opportunity, contradicting the Britain Stronger in Europe campaign's view that Turkey \"is not an issue in this referendum and it shouldn't be\". Vote Leave was accused of implying that 80&nbsp;million Turks would come to the UK if it stayed in the EU. When interviewed in January 2019, he said he had not mentioned Turkey during the campaign.<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-46926119 |title=Brexit: Did Boris Johnson talk Turkey during referendum campaign? |work=BBC News |date=18 January 2019 |access-date=24 January 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite web |url=http://www.voteleavetakecontrol.org/letter_to_the_prime_minister_and_foreign_secretary_getting_the_facts_clear_on_turkey.html |title=Letter to the Prime Minister and Foreign Secretary \u2013 Getting the facts clear on Turkey |date=16 June 2016 |website=VoteLeave |access-date=24 January 2019}}</ref> On 22&nbsp;June 2016, Johnson declared 23&nbsp;June could be \"Britain's independence day\" in a televised debate in front of a 6,000-member audience at [[Wembley Arena]].<ref>{{cite news |last=Wilkinson |first=Michael |date=21 June 2016 |title=EU debate: Boris Johnson says Brexit will be 'Britain's independence day' as Ruth Davidson attacks 'lies' of Leave campaign in front of 6,000-strong Wembley audience |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2016/06/21/eu-debate-boris-johnson-vs-sadiq-khan-in-final-bbc-showdown-with/ |url-status=live |work=[[The Daily Telegraph]] |archive-url=https://archive.today/2016.06.22-165556/http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2016/06/21/eu-debate-boris-johnson-vs-sadiq-khan-in-final-bbc-showdown-with/ |archive-date=22 June 2016 |url-access=limited |access-date=5 May 2021}}</ref> [[David Cameron]], British prime minister at the time, specifically addressed Johnson's claim, publicly stating, \"the idea that our country isn't independent is nonsense. This whole debate demonstrates our sovereignty.\"<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2016/jun/22/brexit-independence-day-claim-nonsense-says-david-cameron |title=Boris Johnson's independence day claim nonsense, says David Cameron |work=[[The Guardian]] |date=22 June 2016}}</ref>\n\nFollowing the victory of the \"Leave\" campaign, Cameron resigned as Conservative leader and prime minister. Johnson was widely regarded as the front-runner to succeed him.<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2016/jun/24/boris-johnson-favourite-to-replace-david-cameron-as-pm-after-brexit |title=Boris Johnson Favourite to replace David Cameron as PM after Brexit |work=The Guardian |location= London |date=24 June 2016 |access-date=30 June 2016}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |last=Cowburn |first=Ashley |date=30 June 2016 |title=Michael Gove's statement on running for |work=The Independent |location=London |access-date=30 June 2016}}</ref> Johnson announced he would not stand in the [[2016 Conservative Party leadership election|Conservative leadership election]].<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-36672591 |title=Boris Johnson rules himself out of Conservative leader race |work=BBC News|date=30 June 2016|access-date=30 June 2016}}</ref> Shortly before this, Michael Gove, hitherto a Johnson ally, concluded that Johnson \"cannot provide the leadership or build the team for the task ahead\".<ref>{{cite news |last=Witte |first=Griff |date=30 June 2016 |title=Stung by a betrayal, former London mayor Boris Johnson ends bid to lead Britain |archive-date=1 July 2016 |access-date=5 May 2021 |url-access=limited}}</ref> ''The Daily Telegraph'' called Gove's comments \"the most spectacular political assassination in a generation\".<ref>{{cite news |last1=Swinford |first1=Steven |last2=Hope |first2=Christopher |last3=Dominiczak |first3=Peter |author2-link=Christopher Hope (journalist) |title=Boris Johnson's allies |work=The Telegraph |date=1 July 2016 |access-date=16 April 2020|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160819030551/http://www.msn.com/en-gb/news/uknews/boris-johnsons-allies-accuse-michael-gove-of-systematic-and-calculated-plot-to-destroy-his-leadership-hopes/ar-AAhPpwa?ocid=spartandhp |archive-date=19 August 2016 |via=MSN News}}</ref> Johnson endorsed [[Andrea Leadsom]]'s candidature, but she dropped out of the race a week later, leaving Theresa May to be elected uncontested.<ref>{{cite news |last1=Asthana |first1=Anushka |last2=Mason |first2=Rowena |date=4 July 2016 |title=Boris Johnson endorses Andrea Leadsom in Tory leadership bid |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2016/jul/04/boris-johnson-endorses-andrea-leadsom-in-tory-leadership-bid |work=The Guardian |location=London |access-date=5 July 2016}}</ref>\n\n===Foreign Secretary: 2016\u20132018===\n{{Main|Boris Johnson's tenure as Foreign Secretary}}\n[[File:Boris Johnson FCA.jpg|thumb|229x229px|Official portrait of Johnson as Foreign Secretary, 2017]]\nAfter May had become Conservative Party leader and prime minister, she appointed Johnson [[Foreign Secretary]] in July 2016.<ref name=\"BloombergFSec\">{{cite news|url=https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2016-07-13/boris-johnson-appointed-u-k-foreign-secretary-in-may-government|title=Boris Johnson Appointed U.K. Foreign Secretary in May Government|last=Hutton|first=Robert|publisher=Bloomberg L.P.|access-date=13 July 2016}}</ref> Analysts saw the appointment as a tactic to weaken Johnson politically: the new positions of \"[[Brexit secretary]]\" and [[Secretary of State for International Trade|international trade secretary]] left the foreign secretary as a figurehead with few powers.<ref name=\"BloombergFSec\"/><ref name=\"GoodForMay\">{{cite news |url=http://www.newstatesman.com/politics/staggers/2016/07/sending-boris-johnson-foreign-office-bad-britain-good-theresa-may |title=Sending Boris Johnson to the Foreign Office is bad for Britain, good for Theresa May |date=14 July 2016 |access-date=14 July 2016 |first=Stephen |last=Bush |work=New Statesman}}</ref> Johnson's appointment ensured he would often be out of the country and unable to organise and mobilise backbenchers against her, while forcing him to take responsibility for problems caused by withdrawing from the EU.<ref>{{cite news |url=http://www.spiegel.de/politik/ausland/boris-johnson-als-britischer-aussenminister-der-muellmann-von-number-10-kommentar-a-1102927.html |title=Boris Johnson als Au\u00dfenminister: Der news|url=http://www.thenational.scot/news/16310206.EU_diplomats_reveal_Boris_Johnson_said__f__k_business__over_Brexit_fears/|title=EU diplomats reveal Boris Johnson said 'f**k business' over Brexit fears|date=23 June 2018|work=The National|access-date=24 June 2018}}</ref><ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-44618154|title=Johnson challenged over 'Brexit expletive'|date=26 June 2018|work=BBC News|access-date=27 June 2018}}</ref>\n\nJohnson said that [[US recognition of Jerusalem as capital of Israel|US recognition of Jerusalem as Israel's capital]] is a \"moment of opportunity\" for peace.<ref>{{cite news |title=Boris Johnson under fire after calling Trump's Jerusalem embassy move a 'moment of opportunity' for peace |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/boris-johnson-trump-jerusalem-israel-palestine-embassy-decision-two-state-solution-criticism-a8174146.html |work=The Independent |date=23 January 2018}}</ref> In June 2018, Johnson accused the [[UNHRC]] of focusing disproportionately on the [[Israeli\u2013Palestinian conflict]] and Israel's [[Israeli-occupied territories|occupation]] of the [[Palestinian territories]].<ref>{{cite news |title=Britain condemns 'anti-Israel bias' at UN Human Rights Council |url=https://www.timesofisrael.com/britain-condemns-anti-israel-bias-at-un-human-rights-council/ |work=[[The Times of Israel]] |date=18 June 2018}}</ref>\n[[File:Boris Johnson with Gordon Brown in London - 2018 (27295267767).jpg|thumb|Johnson with former prime minister [[Gordon Brown]] in May 2018]]\nSecret recordings obtained by ''[[BuzzFeed News]]'' in June 2018 revealed Johnson's dissatisfaction with Prime Minister Theresa May's negotiating style, accusing her of being too collaborative with the European Union in Brexit negotiations. Comparing May's approach to that of the US President Donald Trump \u2013 who at the time was engaged in a combative trade war with the EU because it raised tariffs on metal \u2013 Johnson said: \"Imagine Trump doing Brexit. He'd go in bloody hard&nbsp;... There'd be all sorts of breakdowns, all sorts of chaos. Everyone would think he'd gone mad. But actually you might get somewhere. It's a very, very good thought.\" He also called [[Philip Hammond]] and the Treasury \"the heart of Remain\" and accused individuals of scaremongering over a Brexit \"meltdown\", saying \"No panic. ''[[Pro bono publico]]'', no bloody panic. It's going to be all right in the end.\"<ref>{{cite news |last1=Pitas |first1=Costas |title=Brace for a possible 'Brexit meltdown' but don't panic, Johnson says -BuzzFeed |url=https://www.reuters.com/article/uk-britain-eu-johnson/brace-for-a-possible-brexit-meltdown-but-dont-panic-johnson-says-buzzfeed-idUSKCN1J40LJ |access-date=25 August 2019 |work=Reuters |date=8 June 2018}}</ref>\n\nDuring trips to the United States as foreign secretary, Johnson had repeated meetings with Trump adviser and speechwriter [[Stephen Miller (political advisor)|Stephen Miller]], which were held off White House grounds and kept quiet from May. During the meetings, Miller and Johnson \"swapped speech-writing ideas and tips\".<ref>{{cite news  |work=[[Politico]] |title=Boris and Donald: A very special relationship |date=13 December 2019 |url=https://www.politico.com/news/2019/12/12/trump-boris-johnson-relationship-083732}}</ref>\n\nIn July 2018, three days after the [[Cabinet of the United Kingdom|cabinet]] had its meeting at [[Chequers]] to agree on a [[The future relationship between the United Kingdom and the European Union|Brexit strategy]],<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-44749993 |title=At-a-glance: The new UK Brexit plan agreed at Chequers |date= 7 July 2018 |work=BBC News}}</ref> Johnson, along with Brexit Secretary [[David Davis (British politician)|David Davis]],<ref>{{Cite news |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2018/jul/08/david-davis-resigns-as-brexit-secretary-reports-say |title=Brexit secretary David Davis resigns plunging government into crisis |last=Stewart |first=Heather |date=9 July 2018|work=The Guardian |location= London}}</ref> resigned his post.<ref>{{Cite news |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2018/jul/09/boris-johnson-resigns-as-foreign-secretary-brexit |title=May's plan 'sticks in the throat', says Boris Johnson as he resigns over Brexit |date=9 July 2018 |work=The Guardian |location= London |first1=Heather |last1=Stewart |first2=Pippa |last2=Crerar |first3=Dan |last3=Sabbagh}}</ref>\n\n===Return to the backbenches: 2018\u20132019===\nBy resigning as foreign secretary, Johnson returned to the role of a [[backbench MP]]. In July, he delivered a resignation speech, stating that ministers were \"saying one thing to the EU about what we are really doing, and pretending another to the electorate\". Johnson added that \"it is not too late to save Brexit. We have time in these negotiations. We have changed tack once and we can change once again\".<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-44871629|title=Johnson: It is not too late to save Brexit|date=18 July 2018|work=BBC News|access-date=18 July 2018}}</ref> ''Buzzfeed'' reported Johnson had been in contact with [[Steve Bannon]], [[Donald Trump]]'s former chief adviser. In interviews, Bannon had praised Johnson and said he should challenge Theresa May for the party leadership.<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.buzzfeednews.com/article/lesterfeder/boris-johnson-steve-bannon |title=Boris Johnson Has Been Privately Talking To Steve Bannon As They Plot Their Next Moves |work=Buzzfeed News |date=25 July 2018 |first=J. Lester |last=Feder}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/drone-strikes-are-retribution-for-atrocities-boris-johnson-suggests-p8rg90db7 |title=Drone strikes are retribution for atrocities, Boris Johnson suggests |first=Oliver |last=Wright |date=26 July 2018 |work=The Times |location=London |url-access=subscription }}</ref> In January 2019, Johnson came under criticism for remarks he had made during the 2016 Leave campaign regarding the prospect of Turkish accession to the European Union; he denied making such remarks.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.itv.com/news/2019-01-18/boris-johnson-under-attack-over-turkey-claim/|title=Boris Johnson under attack over Turkey claim|website=[[ITV News]]|date=18 January 2019|access-date=21 January 2019}}</ref> In March 2019, Johnson said that expenditure on investigating historic allegations of child abuse, instead of more police on the streets, was money \"spaffed up the wall\".<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.belfasttelegraph.co.uk/news/uk/boris-johnson-hits-out-at-police-spending-on-historic-child-abuse-probes-37909038.html|title=Boris Johnson hits out at police spending on historic child abuse probes|access-date=13 March 2019|work=[[The Belfast Telegraph]]}}</ref> A victim, anti-abuse organisations, a police chief and Shadow police minister [[Louise Haigh]] strongly criticised this.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-47560192|title=Boris Johnson historical child sex abuse comments 'horrific'|work=BBC News|date=13 March 2019|access-date=13 March 2019}}</ref>\n\n====Journalism====\nIn July 2018, Johnson signed a 12\u2011<!--nb hyphen -->month contract to write articles for the [[Telegraph Media Group]].<ref name=\"gov\">{{cite web|url=https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/attachment_data/file/732866/180808_Boris_Johnson_The_Telegraph.pdf|title=ACoBA Letter to Johnson|date=8 August 2018}}</ref> In August, the [[Advisory Committee on Business Appointments]] (ACoBA) reported that this employment was a breach of the [[Ministerial Code]].<ref name=\"gov\"/><ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-45121976|title=Johnson's Telegraph contract broke rules|date=9 August 2018|work=BBC News|access-date=9 August 2018}}</ref> In December, Johnson was ordered to apologise to Parliament for failing to declare \u00a350,000 of earnings. The [[Parliamentary Commissioner for Standards]] found the errors were not inadvertent, and that Johnson had failed on nine occasions to make declarations within the rules.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.businessinsider.com/boris-johnson-ordered-to-apologise-to-parliament-for-failing-to-declare-earnings-2018-12|title=Boris Johnson ordered to apologise to Parliament for failing to declare earnings|first=Adam|last=Bienkov|website=[[Business Insider]]|access-date=8 December 2018|date=6 December 2018}}</ref>\n\nIn September 2018, Johnson wrote: \"We have opened ourselves to perpetual political blackmail. We have wrapped a suicide vest around the British constitution \u2013 and handed the detonator to [[Michel Barnier]].\" Senior Tories heavily criticised him, with [[Alan Duncan]] of the Foreign Office vowing to ensure the comments marked \"the political end of Boris Johnson\".<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2018/sep/09/boris-johnson-calls-theresa-may-chequers-brexit-plan-suicide-vest|title=Tories condemn Boris Johnson for Brexit 'suicide vest' remarks|first=Peter|last=Walker|date=9 September 2018 |work=The Guardian |access-date=9 September 2018}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/boris-johnson-disgusting-criticism-theresa-may-conservative-row-chequers-plan-suicide-vest-a8529621.html|title=Open warfare between top Tories over Boris Johnson 'suicide vest' jibe at May |work=The Independent |access-date=9 September 2018}}</ref>\n\nIn April 2019, the [[Independent Press Standards Organisation]] ruled that a claim in a 6&nbsp;January 2019 article in ''The Daily Telegraph'', \"The British people won't be scared into backing a woeful Brexit deal nobody voted for\", authored by Johnson,<ref name=\"Telegraph-2019-01\">{{cite news |last1=Johnson |first1=Boris |date=6 January 2019 |title=The British people won't be scared into backing a woeful Brexit deal nobody voted for |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/politics/2019/01/06/british-people-wont-scared-backing-woeful-brexit-deal-nobody/ |url-status=live |work=[[The Daily Telegraph]] |archive-url=https://archive.today/2019.01.07-194346/https://www.telegraph.co.uk/politics/2019/01/06/british-people-wont-scared-backing-woeful-brexit-deal-nobody/ |archive-date=7 January 2019 |access-date=5 May 2021 |url-access=limited}}</ref> that a no-deal [[Brexit]] was \"by some margin preferred by the British public\" was false, and \"represented a failure to take care over the accuracy of the article in breach of Clause 1 (i)\" of its guidelines, and required that a correction to the false claim be published in the print edition, and appended to the online version.<ref name=\"IPSO-2019-04\">{{cite web |title=00154-19 Stirling v The Daily Telegraph |url=https://www.ipso.co.uk/rulings-and-resolution-statements/ruling/?id=00154-19 |publisher=[[Independent Press Standards Organisation]] |access-date=12 April 2019}}</ref>\n\n====2019 Conservative Party leadership election====\n{{main|2019 Conservative Party leadership election}}\n[[File:Back Boris logo.png|thumb|The logo used by Johnson's leadership campaign in 2019]]\nOn 16&nbsp;May 2019, Johnson confirmed he would stand in the forthcoming [[2019 Conservative Party leadership election|Conservative Party leadership election]] following [[Theresa May]]'s anticipated resignation.<ref name=\"Stand\">{{cite news |title=Johnson confirms bid for Tory leadership |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-48299424 |work=BBC News |access-date=28 May 2019 |date=16 May 2019}}</ref> On 7&nbsp;June, Johnson formally launched his campaign, saying, \"we must leave the EU on 31&nbsp;October. We must do better than the current Withdrawal Agreement that has been rejected three times by Parliament\u2014and let me clear that I am not aiming for a no-deal outcome. I don't think that we will end up with any such thing. But it is only responsible to prepare vigorously and seriously for no deal.\"<ref>{{cite web |url=https://blogs.spectator.co.uk/2019/06/full-text-boris-johnson-launches-his-tory-leadership-campaign/ |title=Full text: Boris Johnson launches his Tory leadership campaign |website=The Spectator|date=12 June 2019}}</ref> On the campaign trail, Johnson warned of \"catastrophic consequences for voter trust in politics\" if the government pushed the EU for further delays. He advocated removing the [[Backstop agreement|backstop]] from any Brexit deal and replacing it with alternative arrangements. On 25 and 26&nbsp;August, he announced plans to retain \u00a37 or \u00a39&nbsp;billion of the \u00a339&nbsp;billion divorce payment the UK is due to transfer to the EU upon withdrawal.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/boris-johnson-threatens-eu-over-brexit-divorce-bill-and-raises-general-election-stakes-zwb8xjp6z|title=Boris Johnson threatens EU over Brexit divorce bill and raises general election stakes|work=The Times|last1=Shipman|first1=Tim|last2=Wheeler|first2=Caroline|last3=Urwin|first3=Rosamund|date=25 August 2019|url-access=subscription|access-date=14 April 2020}}</ref><ref>{{cite web |url=https://news.sky.com/story/boris-johnson-chances-of-brexit-deal-improving-now-my-messages-have-landed-11793797 |title=Boris Johnson: 'Chances of Brexit deal improving now my messages have landed' |publisher=[[Sky News]] |last=Heffer |first=Greg |date=26 August 2019 |access-date=14 April 2020}}</ref>\n\nJohnson initially<!-- ?? when ?? ---> pledged to cut income tax for earners of more than \u00a350,000 by raising the 40% tax threshold to \u00a380,000, but backed away from this plan in June 2019 after coming under criticism in a televised [[BBC]] debate.<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/tory-leadership-debate-boris-johnson-no-deal-brexit-rory-stewart-tax-cut-bbc-a8964566.html |title=Boris Johnson backs down on key campaign promises during Tory leadership debate |date=18 June 2019 |website=The Independent |access-date=21 June 2019}}</ref> He also said he planned to raise the level at which low-paid workers start to pay [[National Insurance]].<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-48699393 |title=Tory MPs choosing leadership final two |date=20 June 2019 |access-date=20 June 2019}}</ref>\n\nA poll of party members published on 13&nbsp;June showed Johnson to be the clear front-runner.<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.theweek.co.uk/94729/who-will-be-the-next-prime-minister-the-odds-and-polls |title=The next UK prime minister: the odds and polls |website=The Week UK}}</ref> He received 114 votes in the first ballot of party MPs,<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-48714673 |title='Tactical voting' claim in Tory leader poll |work=BBC News |date=21 June 2019 |access-date=21 June 2019}}</ref> 126 in the next, 143 votes in the third and 157 in the fourth. In the last ballot, on 20&nbsp;June, he reached 160 votes and was named one of the final two candidates, alongside Jeremy Hunt.<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-48711077 |title=Hunt to face Johnson in Tory leader race |work=BBC News |date=20 June 2019 |access-date=21 June 2019}}</ref>\n\nThe members' vote closed on 22&nbsp;July. The following day, Johnson was elected leader with 92,153 votes (66%) to Hunt's 46,656 (34%).<ref>{{cite news |last=Stewart |first=Heather |title=Boris Johnson elected new Tory leader |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/jul/23/boris-johnson-elected-new-tory-leader-prime-minister |access-date=23 July 2019 |work=The Guardian |date=23 July 2019}}</ref>\n\n==Prime minister (2019\u20132022)==\n{{main|Premiership of Boris Johnson}}\n\n===First term (July\u2013December 2019)===\n{{Wikisource|Boris Johnson: First Speech as Prime Minister}}\nOn 24&nbsp;July 2019, the day following Johnson's election as Conservative Party leader, Queen [[Elizabeth&nbsp;II]] accepted [[Theresa May]]'s resignation and appointed Johnson as prime minister. This made Johnson the second prime minister to be born outside the British Isles, after fellow Conservative [[Bonar Law]], and the first to be born outside [[territorial evolution of the British Empire|British territories]].<ref>{{Cite news|author=<!--Staff writer(s); no by-line.-->|url=https://www.theamerican.co.uk/pr/ne-Boris-Johnson-First-US-Born-UK-PM|title=Boris Johnson Becomes First US born Prime Minister of Britain|date=23 July 2019|magazine=[[The American (magazine)|The American]]|location=United Kingdom}}</ref> Johnson appointed [[Dominic Cummings]], whom he worked with on the Vote Leave campaign, as his senior advisor.<ref>{{Cite news |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49101464 |title = Who are Boris Johnson's key advisers?|work = BBC News|date = 30 July 2019}}</ref>\n\n====Brexit policy====\n[[File:Johnson met with Macron for Brexit issue.jpg|thumb|Johnson discussing Brexit with French President [[Emmanuel Macron]] in Paris]]\n[[File:Johnson signed Brexit Withdrawal Agreement.jpg|thumb|Johnson signing the Brexit Withdrawal Agreement]]\nIn his [[s:Boris Johnson: First Speech as Prime Minister|first speech as PM]], Johnson said that the United Kingdom would leave the European Union on 31&nbsp;October 2019 with or [[no deal Brexit|without a deal]], and promised to remove the [[Irish backstop]] from the Brexit withdrawal agreement.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/live/2019/jul/24/boris-johnson-prepares-to-enter-downing-st-and-name-cabinet-theresa-may-prime-minister-live-news|title=Boris Johnson cabinet: Sajid Javid, Priti Patel and Dominic Raab given top jobs \u2013 live news|first=Andrew|last=Sparrow|date=24 July 2019|work=The Guardian}}</ref><ref>{{cite book |last1=Westlake |first1=M. |author-link=Martin Westlake |title=Outside the EU: Options for Britain |date=2020 |publisher=Agenda Publishing |isbn=978-1-78821-312-7 |pages=168\u2013169 |jstor=j.ctv16qjx9d.20}}</ref> Johnson declared his intention to [[Brexit negotiations in 2019|re-open negotiations]] on the withdrawal agreement, but talks did not immediately resume as the EU refused to accept Johnson's pre-condition that the backstop be removed.<ref>{{cite news |last1=Woodcock |first1=Andrews |title=Boris Johnson set for Brexit deadlock after admitting no progress made with EU withdrawal agreement |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/boris-johnson-brexit-eu-withdrawal-agreement-deadline-deal-leave-a9026001.html |access-date=30 July 2021 |work=The Independent |date=29 July 2019 }}</ref> The government subsequently announced \u00a32.1bn of funding to prepare for a no-deal Brexit on 31&nbsp;October.<ref>{{cite news |title=Brexit: \u00a32.1bn extra for no-deal planning |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-49183324 |access-date=30 July 2021 |work=BBC News |date=1 August 2019}}</ref> On 28&nbsp;August 2019, UK and EU negotiators agreed to resume regular meetings to discuss the withdrawal agreement.<ref>{{cite news| url= https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/aug/29/time-to-step-up-tempo-of-brexit-talks-says-bullish-boris-johnson | title = Time to step up tempo of Brexit talks, says bullish Boris Johnson | author= Heather Stewart, Severin Carrell and Jennifer Rankin| newspaper = The Guardian | date = 29 August 2019 | access-date = 30 August 2019}}</ref>\n\nAlso on 28&nbsp;August 2019, Johnson declared he had asked the Queen to [[Prorogation in the United Kingdom|prorogue parliament]] from 10&nbsp;September, narrowing the window in which parliament could block a no-deal Brexit and causing a [[2019 British prorogation controversy|political controversy]].<ref>{{Cite news |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/aug/28/chancellor-sajid-javid-fast-tracked-spending-review-fuels-talk-of-early-election |title=Boris Johnson asks Queen to suspend parliament |last1=Elgot |first1=Jessica |date=28 August 2019 |work=The Guardian |access-date=28 August 2019 |last2=Stewart |first2=Heather |issn=0261-3077}}</ref> The Queen at [[Privy Council of the United Kingdom|Privy Council]] approved prorogation later the same day, and it began on 10&nbsp;September, scheduled to last until 14&nbsp;October.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/live/2019/aug/28/spending-review-set-for-next-week-fuels-election-speculation-live?page=with:block-5d668a668f08ea59f447a9f1#block-5d668a668f08ea59f447a9f1|title=Brexit: Queen consents to suspension of parliament for five weeks \u2013 live news|date=28 August 2019|work=The Guardian|access-date=28 August 2019}}</ref> Some suggested<ref>{{cite news |last1=Taylor |first1=Simon |last2=Graham |first2=Chris |title='A very British coup': How Europe reacted to Boris Johnson suspending parliament in Brexit push |url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/politics/2019/08/28/backstop-wont-withdrawn-back-promise-say-eu-leaders/ |archive-url=https://ghostarchive.org/archive/20220110/https://www.telegraph.co.uk/politics/2019/08/28/backstop-wont-withdrawn-back-promise-say-eu-leaders/ |archive-date=10 January 2022 |url-status=live |access-date=5 May 2021 |work=The Telegraph |date=29 August 2019 |url-access=limited}}{{cbignore}}</ref> that this prorogation amounted to a [[self-coup]], and on 31&nbsp;August 2019, protests occurred in towns and cities throughout the United Kingdom.<ref>{{cite news |title=Parliament suspension: Thousands protest across the UK |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-49534940 |access-date=2 September 2019 |publisher=BBC |date=31 August 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |last1=Ferguson |first1=Donna |last2=Murphy |first2=Simon |last3=Townsend |first3=Mark |last4=Wall |first4=Tom |title=From Bodmin to Berlin, crowds vent their fury at Boris Johnson's 'coup' |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/aug/31/bodmin-to-berlin-crowds-vent-fury-boris-johnson-coup |access-date=2 September 2019 |work=[[The Observer]] |date=31 August 2019}}</ref> As of 2&nbsp;September 2019, three separate court cases challenging Johnson's action were in progress or scheduled to take place,<ref>{{cite news |last1=MacAskill |first1=Andrew |last2=James |first2=William |title=Factbox: UK government ensnared in court battles over suspending parliament |url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-britain-eu-courtcases-factbox-idUSKCN1VK1LK |access-date=2 September 2019 |work=Reuters |date=30 August 2019}}</ref> and on 11&nbsp;September, three Scottish judges ruled the prorogation of the UK Parliament to be unlawful.<ref>{{cite news |last1=Carrell |first1=Severin |title=Scottish judges rule Boris Johnson's prorogation unlawful |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/sep/11/scottish-judges-rule-boris-johnsons-prorogation-unlawful |access-date=11 September 2019 |work=The Guardian |date=11 September 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |title=Brexit: Scottish judges rule Parliament suspension is unlawful |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-scotland-49661855 |access-date=11 September 2019 |work=BBC News |date=11 September 2019}}</ref> On 12&nbsp;September, Johnson denied lying to the Queen over suspension of the parliament, while a Belfast Court rejected claims that his Brexit plans will have a negative impact on Northern Ireland's peace policy.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://cbs6albany.com/news/nation-world/johnson-denies-lying-to-queen-court-rejects-brexit-case|title=Johnson denies lying to queen; court rejects Brexit case|access-date=12 September 2019|website=6News|date=12 September 2019}}</ref> On 24 September, the Supreme Court ruled unanimously that Johnson's advice to prorogue parliament was unlawful, and therefore the prorogation was rendered null and of no effect.<ref>{{cite news |last= Bowcott |first=Owen |date=24 September 2019 |title=Boris Johnson's suspension of parliament unlawful, supreme court rules |url=https://www.theguardian.com/law/2019/sep/24/boris-johnsons-suspension-of-parliament-unlawful-supreme-court-rules-prorogue |work=[[The Guardian]] |location=London |access-date=24 September 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |title=Supreme Court: Suspending Parliament was unlawful, judges rule |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49810261 |work=[[BBC News]] |location=London |date=24 September 2019 |access-date=24 September 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite court |litigants=R (Miller) (Appellant) v The Prime Minister (Respondent) and Cherry & Ors |access-date=24 September 2018}}</ref>\n\nWhen parliament resumed on 3&nbsp;September 2019, Johnson indicated he would call a [[general election]] under the [[Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011|Fixed-term Parliaments Act]] after opposition and rebel Conservative MPs successfully voted against the government to take control of the order of business to prevent a no-deal exit.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/politics/2019/09/03/brexit-latest-news-boris-johnson-no-deal-vote-general-election/ |archive-url=https://ghostarchive.org/archive/20220110/https://www.telegraph.co.uk/politics/2019/09/03/brexit-latest-news-boris-johnson-no-deal-vote-general-election/ |archive-date=10 January 2022 |url-status=live|title=Brexit vote result: Boris Johnson demands general election after rebel MPs seize control of Commons agenda|last1=Rayner|first1=Gordon|date=3 September 2019|work=The Telegraph|access-date=5 May 2021|last2=Sheridan|first2=Danielle |url-access=limited|issn=0307-1235}}{{cbignore}}</ref> Despite government opposition, the [[Benn Act]], a bill to block a no-deal exit, passed the Commons on 4&nbsp;September 2019, causing Johnson to propose a general election on 15&nbsp;October.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-49580185|title=MPs back bill aimed at blocking no-deal Brexit|date=4 September 2019|access-date=5 September 2019}}</ref> His motion was unsuccessful as it failed to command the support of two-thirds of the House.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-49584907|title=Johnson's call for general election rejected by MPs|date=4 September 2019|access-date=5 September 2019}}</ref>\n\nIn October 2019, following bilateral talks between Johnson and [[Taoiseach]] [[Leo Varadkar]],<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49995133|title=Brexit: Boris Johnson and Leo Varadkar 'can see pathway to a deal'|work=BBC News|date=10 October 2019|access-date=23 December 2019}}</ref> the UK and EU agreed to a [[Brexit withdrawal agreement#Revisions in 2019|revised deal]], which [[Brexit and the Irish border#2019 renegotiation: New protocol|replaced the backstop]] with a new [[Northern Ireland Protocol]].<ref name=\"bbcNewDeal\">{{cite web|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-50083026|title=Brexit: What is in Boris Johnson's new deal with the EU?|work=BBC News|date=21 October 2019|access-date=23 December 2019}}</ref><ref name=\"bbcNewDeal2\">{{cite web|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-50079385|title=Brexit: EU and UK reach deal but DUP refuses support|work=BBC News|date=17 October 2019|access-date=23 December 2019}}</ref><ref name=\"FTNewDeal\">{{cite news|url=https://www.ft.com/content/cdb2dadc-ec40-11e9-a240-3b065ef5fc55|title= How Boris Johnson moved to break the Brexit deadlock|website=Financial Times|date=11 October 2019|access-date=23 December 2019|last1=Parker|first1=George|last2=Brunsden|first2=Jim|url-access=subscription}}</ref>\n\nIn December 2019, Johnson said: \"quite a large number of people coming in from the whole of the EU{{emdash}}580 million population{{emdash}}[had been] able to treat the UK as though it's basically part of their own country and the problem with that is there has been no control at all\".<ref>{{cite news|date=9 December 2019|title=Boris Johnson says EU migrants have been 'treating UK as part of their own country' for too long|work=[[The Irish Post]]|url=https://www.irishpost.com/news/boris-johnson-pledges-stop-eu-migrants-treating-uk-part-country-174975}}</ref> The co-founder of [[The 3&nbsp;Million]] accused Johnson of \"demonising\" EU migrants.<ref>{{cite news|date=9 December 2019|title=Boris Johnson faces backlash and claims of racism after saying migrants should not 'treat UK as their own'|work=The Independent|url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/home-news/boris-johnson-eu-migrants-immigration-britain-general-election-a9238941.html}}</ref>\n\n====First Cabinet====\n{{main|First Johnson ministry}}\n[[File:Johnson's 1st cabinet meeting.jpg|thumb|Johnson holding his first cabinet meeting at [[10 Downing Street]], 25 July 2019]]\nJohnson [[2019 British cabinet formation|appointed]] his [[First Johnson ministry|Cabinet]] on 24&nbsp;July 2019,<ref name=\"BBC25\">{{cite news |title=Boris Johnson: Does his cabinet reflect 'modern Britain'? |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-49034735 |access-date=25 July 2019 |work=BBC News |date=25 July 2019}}</ref> dismissing 11 senior ministers and accepting the resignation of six others.<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/boris-johnson-the-godfather-takes-his-retribution-in-massacre-of-cabinet-ministers-zxr5vvk00 |url-access=subscription|title=Boris Johnson the Godfather takes his retribution in massacre of cabinet ministers |last1=Swinford |first1=Steven |last2=Chorley |first2=Matt |work=The Times |date=25 July 2019 |access-date=27 July 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/jul/24/philip-hammond-quits-johnson-fully-aligned-chancellor-gauke-stewart |title='Summer's day massacre' may spell backbench trouble for Boris Johnson |last=Syal |first=Rajeev |work=The Guardian |date=24 July 2019 |access-date=27 July 2019}}</ref> The mass dismissal was the most extensive postwar Cabinet reorganisation without a change in the ruling party, exceeding the seven Cabinet ministers dismissed in the \"[[Night of the Long Knives (1962)|Night of the Long Knives]]\" of 1962.<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/boris-johnson-cabinet-reshuffle-ministers-brexit-a9019726.html |last=Woodcock |first=Andrew |title=Boris Johnson dismantles cabinet in reshuffle, building government around people who delivered Brexit vote |work=Independent |date=25 July 2019 |access-date=27 July 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.theguardian.com/media/2019/jul/25/cabinet-massacre-what-the-papers-say-about-boris-johnsons-arrival-in-no-10 |last=Lyons |first=Kate |title='Cabinet massacre': what the papers say about Boris Johnson's arrival in No 10 |work=The Guardian |date=25 July 2019 |access-date=27 July 2019}}</ref>\n\nAmong other appointments, Johnson made [[Dominic Raab]] the [[First Secretary of State]] and foreign secretary, and appointed [[Sajid Javid]] and [[Priti Patel]] as the [[Chancellor of the Exchequer]] and [[Home Secretary]], respectively. Johnson increased the number of ministers attending the Cabinet to 33, four more than had attended the [[Second May ministry|May Cabinet]]. One quarter of those appointed were women, and the Cabinet set a record for ethnic minority representation, with four secretaries of state and two additional ministers coming from minority backgrounds. Nearly two-thirds of those appointed went to fee-paying schools, and almost half had attended [[Oxbridge]].<ref name=\"BBC25\" /> Johnson also created a new ministerial role to be held by himself, [[Minister for the Union]], fulfilling a campaign pledge he had made in the leadership election.<ref>{{cite news |last=Murphy |first=Sean |date=26 July 2019 |title=Boris Johnson gives himself 'Minister for the Union' title |url=https://www.scotsman.com/news/politics/boris-johnson-gives-himself-minister-for-the-union-title-1-4972145 |work=[[The Scotsman]] |archive-url=https://archive.today/2019.07.26-200301/https://www.scotsman.com/news/politics/boris-johnson-gives-himself-minister-for-the-union-title-1-4972145 |archive-date=26 July 2019 |access-date=5 May 2021}}</ref>\n\n====Spending plans====\nShortly after he had become prime minister, Johnson's government announced increased public sector spending. In particular, it was announced that an extra 20,000 police officers would be hired, the roll-out of high-speed broadband would be sped up, the funding per school pupil would be increased to a minimum of \u00a35,000 and \u00a31.8&nbsp;billion for upgrades and new equipment at hospitals. \u00a31&nbsp;billion of the money for hospitals was money that NHS providers had saved over the past three years and then previously been told they could not spend, rather than being new money.<ref>{{cite news |title=Do Boris Johnson's tax and spending plans add up? |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49097133 |work=BBC News |date=5 August 2019 |author=Reality Check Team}}</ref> The Chancellor [[Sajid Javid]] also announced that the spending review would be fast-tracked to September. Javid said that this was so that departments would be free to plan for the planned Brexit date of 31&nbsp;October 2019, but there was speculation that the increased spending was to gain popularity in preparation for a possible election in autumn 2019.<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-boris-johnson-fast-track-spending-review-sajid-javid-a9047781.html |title=Brexit: Boris Johnson fuels early election speculation as chancellor fast-tracks spending review |last=Buchan |first=Lizzy |date=8 August 2019 |work=The Independent}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2019-08-04/johnson-s-spending-fuels-u-k-election-talk-after-majority-cut |title=Johnson's Spending Puts U.K. Parties on Alert for Snap Election |last=Biggs |first=Stuart |work=Bloomberg.com |date=4 August 2019 |publisher=Bloomberg}}</ref>\n\n====Loss of working majority====\nOn 3&nbsp;September 2019, [[Phillip Lee (politician)|Phillip Lee]] [[crossed the floor]] to the [[Liberal Democrats (UK)|Liberal Democrats]] following a disagreement with Johnson's [[Brexit]] policy. This left the government without a [[working majority]] in the House of Commons.<ref>{{cite news |title=Boris Johnson loses majority after Tory MP defects during speech |url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/philip-lee-tory-lib-dem-defection-boris-johnson-majority-brexit-commons-a9090356.html |access-date=3 September 2019 |work=The Independent |date=3 September 2019}}</ref> Later that day, [[September 2019 suspension of rebel Conservative MPs|21 Conservative MPs]], including the [[Father of the House (United Kingdom)|Father of the House]] and former Chancellor [[Kenneth Clarke]], and another former Chancellor [[Philip Hammond]], had the party [[Whip (politics)|whip]] withdrawn for defying party orders and supporting an opposition motion.<ref>{{cite web |url=https://www.politicshome.com/news/uk/political-parties/conservative-party/news/106279/twenty-one-tory-rebels-lose-party-whip |title=Twenty-one Tory rebels lose party whip after backing bid to block no-deal Brexit |date=4 September 2019 |website=PoliticsHome.com |access-date=5 September 2019}}</ref> (The whip was restored to 10 former Conservative ministers on 29&nbsp;October.<ref>{{cite web |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-50221856 |title=Restoration of Conservative Whip |date=29 October 2019 |work=BBC News }}</ref>)\n[[File:Boris Johnson after 2019 General election.png|thumb|Johnson giving a speech in December 2019 after the [[2019 United Kingdom general election|2019 general election]]]]\nOn 5&nbsp;September 2019, Johnson's brother [[Jo Johnson]] resigned from the government and announced that he would step down as MP, describing his position as \"torn between family and national interest\".<ref name=\"bbc2\">{{cite news |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-49594793 |title=PM's brother quits as Tory MP and minister |date=5 September 2019 |access-date=5 September 2019 |work=BBC News}}</ref> Two days later, [[Amber Rudd]] resigned as [[Secretary of State for Work and Pensions]] and from the Conservative Party, describing the withdrawal of the party whip from the MPs as an \"assault on decency and democracy\".<ref>{{cite news |last=Shipman |first=Tim |date=7 September 2019 |url=https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/exclusive-amber-rudd-resigns-from-cabinet-and-quits-tories-06dqxcjct |title=Exclusive: Amber Rudd resigns from cabinet and quits Tories |work=[[The Sunday Times]] |issn=0140-0460 |access-date=5 May 2021 |url-access=limited}}</ref>\n\n====2019 general election====\n{{main|2019 United Kingdom general election}}\nIn October 2019, Parliament was dissolved, and an election called for 12&nbsp;December. The election resulted in the Conservative Party winning 43.6% of the vote and a parliamentary landslide majority of 80 seats\u2014its biggest since [[1987 United Kingdom general election|1987]] under [[Margaret Thatcher]].<ref>{{cite news |work=BBC News |title=General Election 2019: What's behind the Conservative victory? |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/election-2019-50774061}}</ref> A key slogan used in the Conservative campaign was their promise to \"[[Get Brexit Done]]\".<ref>{{cite news |last1=Perrigo |first1=Billy |title='Get Brexit Done.' The Slogan That Won Britain's Election |url=https://time.com/5749478/get-brexit-done-slogan-uk-election/ |access-date=11 December 2021 |magazine=Time |date=13 December 2019 }}</ref>\n\n===Second term (December 2019 \u2013 September 2022)===\n====Second Cabinet====\n{{main|Second Johnson ministry|2020 British cabinet reshuffle|2021 British cabinet reshuffle}}\n[[File:Chancellor announces additional support to protect businesses.jpg|thumb|Johnson appointed [[Rishi Sunak]] (right) as [[Chancellor of the Exchequer]] in the [[2020 British cabinet reshuffle|February 2020 cabinet reshuffle]]]]\nJohnson [[2020 British cabinet reshuffle|reshuffled his cabinet]] on 13 February 2020.<ref>{{Cite news |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-51491662 |title=Sajid Javid resigns as chancellor |date=13 February 2020 |work=BBC News |access-date=13 February 2020 }}</ref> Five Cabinet ministers were sacked, including the [[Secretary of State for Northern Ireland|Northern Ireland Secretary]] [[Julian Smith (politician)|Julian Smith]], a decision that was criticised by several politicians and commentators following his success in restoring the [[Northern Ireland Executive]] devolved government.<ref name=\"Walker\">{{cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2020/feb/13/julian-smith-sacked-as-boris-johnson-reshuffles-cabinet|title=Smith, Leadsom and McVey out as Johnson reshuffles cabinet|date=13 February 2020|author=Walker, Peter|work=The Guardian}}</ref> [[Chancellor of the Exchequer]] [[Sajid Javid]] resigned from the Cabinet after refusing a demand from Johnson and [[Dominic Cummings]] that he dismiss his advisers.<ref name=\"JavResign\">{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-51491662|title=Cabinet reshuffle: Sajid Javid resigns as chancellor|website=BBC News|date=13 February 2020}}</ref> Javid was replaced as Chancellor by [[Rishi Sunak]]; Javid later returned to Johnson's Cabinet as [[Secretary of State for Health and Social Care]] in June 2021 following the resignation of [[Matt Hancock]].\n\nJohnson conducted another reshuffle of his cabinet in September 2021. Changes included the dismissal of [[Secretary of State for Education|Education Secretary]] [[Gavin Williamson]] who had received significant criticism for his handling of disruption to education, such as the [[2020 UK GCSE and A-Level grading controversy|2020 exam grading controversy]], during the COVID-19 pandemic. [[Dominic Raab]] was moved from [[Foreign Secretary]] to [[Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom|Deputy Prime Minister]] and [[Secretary of State for Justice|Justice Secretary]], replacing [[Robert Buckland]] in the latter role. Raab was replaced as Foreign Secretary by [[Liz Truss]].<ref>{{Cite news|date=15 September 2021|title=Cabinet reshuffle: Raab moved from foreign secretary to deputy PM|work=BBC News|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-58571935|access-date=15 September 2021}}</ref><ref>{{Cite web|last=Heffer|first=Greg|date=15 September 2021|title=Cabinet reshuffle: Gavin Williamson sacked and Dominic Raab moved from foreign secretary|url=https://news.sky.com/story/cabinet-reshuffle-gavin-williamson-departs-education-department-as-dominic-raab-demoted-to-justice-secretary-12408644|access-date=15 September 2021|publisher=Sky News}}</ref>\n\n====COVID-19 pandemic====\n{{main|British government response to the COVID-19 pandemic|COVID-19 vaccination programme in the United Kingdom}}\n{{See also|COVID-19 contracts in the United Kingdom|COVID-19 pandemic in the United Kingdom}}\n\n[[File:Boris Johnson giving a COVID-19 press conference.jpg|thumb|Johnson delivering a press conference on [[COVID-19]], 31 July 2020]]\nThe [[COVID-19 pandemic in the United Kingdom|COVID-19 pandemic]] emerged as a serious crisis within the first few months of Johnson's second term.<ref>{{cite news |last1=Stewart |date=20 March 2020 |work=The Guardian |access-date=20 March 2020 |last2=Walker |first2=Peter |issn=0261-3077}}</ref> Throughout the pandemic, Johnson made a number of policy decisions to curb the pandemic some time after the [[Scientific Advisory Group for Emergencies|Scientific Advisory Group for Emergencies (SAGE)]] advised them, and contradicting his previous pledges and statements.<ref Johnson's non-attendance of five [[Cabinet Office Briefing Rooms|COBR]] briefings during the early months of the pandemic and the failure of the UK government to prepare for and control the outbreak has been criticised.<ref>{{Cite news|last3=Leake|first3=Jonathan|last2=Arbuthnot|first2=George |last1=Calvert|first1=Jonathan |title=Coronavirus: 38 days when Britain sleepwalked into |last28=Fu|first28=H. |last29=Gaythorpe|first29=K. |last30=Geidelberg|first30=L. |display-authors=1}}</ref> This response is thought by some scientists to have contributed to the UK's high death toll from COVID-19, which {{as of|2021|1|lc=y}} was among the highest in the world in total and by population.<ref name=\"reuters_2\">{{Cite news|last=Sandle |first=Andrew MacAskill, Paul|date=25 January
